Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Categorical Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the categorical syllogism is either useless or needlessly lengthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the categorical syllogism is redundant when premises are self-evident, and evaluate its implications for the epistemological status of syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
If premises are self-evident, the syllogism is unnecessary; if not, it is a lengthy chain – either way, useless.

Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the very heart of Aristotelian logic: the categorical syllogism. He argues that this celebrated deductive form either does no intellectual work or burdens the mind with needless complexity. In this lesson, you will dissect his critique and see why he dismisses the syllogistic method as a waste of time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma
Find where he states that if premises are self-evident, then... and if not, then... This is the core of his argument.
He uses the term 'badīhī' for self-evident.
2
Examine his example syllogism
Look at the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is wine; every wine is unlawful; therefore every intoxicant is unlawful.' Identify its premises and conclusion.
Ibn Taymiyya notes that this example is for illustration, even if not all premises are actually self-evident.
3
Analyze the self-evidence claim
According to Ibn Taymiyya, if both premises are self-evident, the conclusion is also self-evident without needing the syllogistic arrangement. Why would that be?
He argues that knowledge of the individuals directly entails the conclusion.
4
Evaluate the epistemological consequence
If premises are not self-evident, they require evidence (like Sunnah and consensus). Then the syllogism is just a long way to state what is already known. What does Ibn Taymiyya conclude about the logicians' method?
He quotes his famous line: 'wasting of time, abundance of nonsense, and wearying of minds.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ينتقل ابن تيمية إلى توجيه نقد آخر للقياس الشمولي ، يحاول أن يبين به أن هذا القياس لا فائدة منه أو هو تطويل لا داعي له . إن المناطقة يقررون أن القياس هو العلم بثبوت الحكم لكل فرد من الأفراد . ولا بد أن يكون ثبوت بعض الأحكام لبعض بديهياً ، أي لا بد أن تكون بعض المقدمات بديهية وإلا انتهى آخر المقدمات إلى الدور . فاذا كانت كل المقدمات بديهية أو بعض المقدمات بديهية ، فإن العلم ببديهية أفرادها أقوى وأسهل ولا حاجة للنظم القياسي ، ويعطي ابن تيمية المثال الآتي : لنفرض المقدمتين البديهيتين « كل مسكر خمر » وكل خمر حرام ، وان لم تكن هذه بديهياً لكن المقصود التمثيل ليعلم بالمثال : حكم سائر القضايا . فاذا قدرنا أننا نعلم بالبديهة أن كل فرد من أفراد الخمر حرام ، ونعلم بالبديهة أن كل فرد من أفراد المسكر خمر ، كان علمنا بالبديهة أن هذه الأفراد حرام من أسهل الأشياء . ويرى أبن تيمية أن ذلك انما يخفى على أكثر الناس ، لكون المقدمات غير بديهية ، بل تكون مبينة بغيرها ، كما في المثال السابق الذي أورده ، فإن المقدمة الأولى ثابتة بالسنة الصحيحة ، والثانية ثابتة بالنص والإجماع . ولكن إذا كانت المقدمتان بديهيتين فعلاً فلا حاجة لنا إلى القياس إن العلم بالنتيجة لا يحتاج إلى هذا النظم القياسي ، إن منطقهم « يعطي تضييع الزمان . وكثرة الهذيان ، واتعاب الأذهان »(۱). (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد ص ٣٦١ .
Then Ibn Taymiyya moves to direct another critique of the categorical syllogism, attempting to show in it that this syllogism is either useless or an unnecessary prolongation. The logicians maintain that the syllogism is the knowledge of the establishment of the judgment for every individual among individuals. And it is necessary that the establishment of some judgments for others be self-evident, i.e., some premises must be self-evident, otherwise the end of the premises would lead to circularity. So if all premises are self-evident or some premises are self-evident, then the knowledge of the self-evidence of their individuals is stronger and easier, and there is no need for the syllogistic form. And Ibn Taymiyya gives the following example: Let us suppose the two self-evident premises: 'Every intoxicant is wine' and 'Every wine is unlawful', even if these are not self-evident, but the purpose is illustration to learn by example the ruling of other propositions. So if we assume that we know by self-evidence that every individual of wine is unlawful, and we know by self-evidence that every individual of intoxicant is wine, then our knowledge by self-evidence that these individuals are unlawful is among the easiest things. And Ibn Taymiyya sees that this is only hidden from most people because the premises are not self-evident, but are explained by others, as in the previous example he gave, for the first premise is established by authentic Sunnah, and the second is established by text and consensus. But if the two premises are actually self-evident, then we have no need for the syllogism; indeed knowledge of the conclusion does not require this syllogistic form. Their logic 'gives wasting of time, abundance of nonsense, and wearying of minds.' (1) Ibn Taymiyya: al-Radd p. 361.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the categorical syllogism (al-qiyās al-shumūlī). He argues that if the premises are self-evident (badīhiyya), then the syllogistic form is unnecessary because the conclusion is directly known; if they are not self-evident, then the syllogism is an unnecessarily lengthy procedure. He illustrates this with an example: the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is wine; every wine is unlawful; therefore every intoxicant is unlawful.' According to Ibn Taymiyya, if both premises are self-evident, the conclusion is equally self-evident without needing the syllogistic arrangement. In reality, most people are unaware of this because the premises are not self-evident; they require evidence from revelation (Sunnah and consensus). But if they were self-evident, the syllogism would be redundant. He concludes with a strong statement: the logicians' method 'wastes time, increases nonsense, and wearies minds.' This critique challenges the epistemological necessity of the Aristotelian syllogism, asserting that it does not produce new knowledge beyond what is already implicit in the premises.

Key Terms
القياس الشمولي
بديهي
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The syllogism becomes more certain.
B) The syllogism is unnecessary because the conclusion is also self-evident.
C) The syllogism must be reformulated.
D) The syllogism is then acceptable.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique challenges the epistemological necessity of the categorical syllogism by showing that it is either redundant (when premises are self-evident) or reduces to a needlessly lengthy process (when premises are not self-evident).

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Syllogistic Necessity
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of syllogism based on self-evidence and the regress problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that syllogism is unnecessary when premises are self-evident and that requiring syllogism for every universal leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
If premises are self-evident, syllogism is redundant; if not, you get an infinite regress.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational assumption of Aristotelian logic: that syllogism is the only path to certain knowledge. He uses the example of khamr to show that if the premises are self-evident, the conclusion follows immediately without formal syllogistic arrangement. Moreover, he argues that requiring a syllogism for every universal proposition leads to an infinite regress, so some universals must be known directly from particulars. This lesson guides you through unpacking this critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example and the claim about self-evidence
Ibn Taymiyya gives the example: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden.' He says if both premises are self-evident (badihiyyan), the conclusion is obvious without a syllogism.
2
Extract the regress argument
If knowledge of a universal proposition always requires a syllogism, and every syllogism requires a universal premise, then we need a universal to know a universal—leading to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, some universals must be known without syllogism.
3
Recognize the epistemological shift
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that universals are derived from particulars via sensory experience, not prior to them. This reverses the priority of universals in Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البديهيتين « كل مسكر خمر » وكل خمر حرام ، وان لم تكن هذه بديهياً لكن المقصود التمثيل ليعلم بالمثال : حكم سائر القضايا . فاذا قدرنا أننا نعلم بالبديهة أن كل فرد من أفراد الخمر حرام ، ونعلم بالبديهة أن كل فرد من أفراد المسكر خمر ، كان علمنا بالبديهة أن هذه الأفراد حرام من أسهل الأشياء . ويرى أبن تيمية أن ذلك انما يخفى على أكثر الناس ، لكون المقدمات غير بديهية ، بل تكون مبينة بغيرها ، كما في المثال السابق الذي أورده ، فإن المقدمة الأولى ثابتة بالسنة الصحيحة ، والثانية ثابتة بالنص والإجماع . ولكن إذا كانت المقدمتان بديهيتين فعلاً فلا حاجة لنا إلى القياس إن العلم بالنتيجة لا يحتاج إلى هذا النظم القياسي ، إن منطقهم « يعطي تضييع الزمان . وكثرة الهذيان ، واتعاب الأذهان » . ويرى ابن تيمية أن من القضايا الكلية ما يمكن العلم به بغير توسط القياس ان الحس يدرك الجزئيات أولاً ، ثم ينتقل الى القضايا العامة ، إننا نرى هذا الإنسان وهذا الإنسان ، وهذا الإنسان ، ونرى أن هذا حساس متحرك بالإرادة ناطق/ وهذا كذا/ وهذا ، فنقضي قضاء عاماً على كل إنسان حساس متحرك بالإرادة . فالعلم بالقضية العامة إما أن يكون بواسطة قياس أو بغير واسطته . فإن كان بواسطة قياس ، والقياس لا بد فيه من قضية كلية ، لزم أن لا يعلم الكلي إلا بالكلي . وذلك يستلزم الدور أو التسلسل . فلا بد أن ينتهي إلى قضية كلية عامة بديهية . واذا أمكن علم إحدى مقدمتي القياس أمكن علم الأخرى سواء أكانت المقدمة الأخرى ، أو النتيجة بغير قياس بالبداهة قد نعلمها أيضاً بالبديهة أو نعلمها متواترة أو مجربة أو مشهورة أو محسوسة . أو مستدلة بالتمثيل أو بالإستقراء كما رأينا . وليس التمثيل ظنياً ، والقياس يقينياً/ بل في التمثيل يقيني ومظنون ، كما في القياس . ويستعيد هنا ابن تيمية ما سبق أن قرره في المقام السالب من أن قياس التمثيل وقياس الشمول واحد ، بل انه يورد قول أكثر الأصوليين : إن مسمى القياس هو قياس التمثيل فقط . ولكنه هو يرى أن الإثنين واحد وذلك أن قياس الشمول : إنما يعني اشتراك الأفراد في الحكم العام وشموله لها ، وقياس التمثيل هو اشتراك اثنين في الحكم الذي يعمهما . ومآل الإثنين واحد ، ومناط الحكم في قياس التمثيل ، أي الجامع والمشترك بين الأصل والفرع هو الكلي في قياس الشمول . بل إن قياس التمثيل هو الأصل الحقيقي لقياس الشمول « إن التجربة إنما دلت على أشياء معينة ، لم تدل على أمر عام . ثم يأتي العقل فيعلم أن المناط هو القدر المشترك بما يعلم من انتفاء ما سواه ، أو مناسبته أو بعلم مناسبته . . فالعقل بحسب إحساسه بالجزئيات ، يدرك بينها قدراً مشتركاً كلياً ، والكليات لا تقع في النفس إلا بعد معرفة الجزئيات المعينة . يقول ابن تيمية « ان معرفة الجزئيات المعينة من أعظم الاسباب في معرفة الكليات ، فكيف يكون ذكرها مضعفاً للقياس ويكون عدم ذكرها موجباً لقوته ويرى أن هذه بخاصة العقل ، فإن خاصيته معرفة الكليات بتوسط معرفته بالجزئيات ، فمن أنكرها ، أنكر خاصة عقل الإنسان » . والأقيسة التي يستعملها الفلاسفة والمناطقة ويجعلونها كلية كلها إنما يعتضدون فيها بالأمثلة وهم يستندون فيها على صفات الأمور المشاهدة ثم يقيسون الغائب على الشاهد بالجامع المشترك الذي يجعلونه كلياً . يقول ابن تيمية ، فإن لم يكن هذا صحيحاً لم يكن مع أحد من أهل الأرض علم كلي ، يشترك فيه ما شهده وما غاب عنه حتى قوله « الخبز يشبع . . والماء يروي » ونحو ذلك . فلم يعلم بحسه الا أموراً معنية ، فمن أين له أن الغائب بمنزلة الشاهد إلا بهذه الطريقة ؟ . والإنسان قد ينكر أمراً حتى يرى واحداً من جنسه ، فيقر بالنوع ، ويستفيد بذلك حكماً كلياً .
The two self-evident premises: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden', even if these are not self-evident, but the purpose is illustration so that the ruling of other propositions may be known by the example. So if we suppose that we know by intuition that every individual of the individuals of khamr is forbidden, and we know by intuition that every individual of the individuals of intoxicant is khamr, then our knowledge by intuition that these individuals are forbidden is among the easiest of things. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is only hidden from most people because the premises are not self-evident, but rather are proven by other things, as in the previous example he gave, for the first premise is established by the authentic Sunnah, and the second by text and consensus. But if the two premises are actually self-evident, then we have no need for syllogism; knowledge of the conclusion does not require this syllogistic arrangement. Their logic 'wastes time, increases rambling, and wearies the minds'. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that among universal propositions, there are those that can be known without the mediation of syllogism. The senses perceive particulars first, then one moves to general propositions. We see this human and this human and this human, and we see that this one is sentient, mobile by will, rational, and this one such-and-such, and this one, so we pass a universal judgment on every human: sentient, mobile by will. So knowledge of the universal proposition is either by means of syllogism or without its mediation. If by syllogism, and the syllogism must include a universal proposition, then it would follow that the universal is not known except by the universal. This necessitates circularity or infinite regress. So it must end with a universal general self-evident proposition. And if knowledge of one of the two premises of a syllogism is possible, then knowledge of the other is possible, whether that other premise or the conclusion is without syllogism by intuition; we may know it also by intuition, or by tawatur, experience, widespread reports, or sensation. Or it may be inferred by analogy or induction as we have seen. And analogy is not merely probable while syllogism is certain; rather, in analogy there is certainty and probability, just as in syllogism. Here Ibn Taymiyya reiterates what he previously established in the negative context: that analogical inference (qiyas al-tamthil) and categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) are one. Indeed, he cites the view of most usulis that the name qiyas applies only to analogical inference. But he himself holds that the two are one. That is because categorical syllogism only means the sharing of individuals in a general ruling and its inclusion of them, while analogical inference is the sharing of two things in the ruling that encompasses them both. The outcome of both is one, and the basis of the ruling in analogical inference, i.e., the commonality and shared aspect between the original and the branch, is the universal in the categorical syllogism. Rather, analogical inference is the true origin of the categorical syllogism. 'Experience has only indicated specific things; it has not indicated a general matter. Then the intellect comes to know that the basis is the shared aspect, by knowing the negation of what is other than it, or its appropriateness, or by knowing its appropriateness... So the intellect, through its sense of particulars, perceives among them a universal shared aspect, and universals do not occur in the soul except after knowledge of specific particulars.' Ibn Taymiyya says: 'Knowledge of specific particulars is among the greatest causes of knowledge of universals; so how could mentioning them weaken the syllogism, and non-mention of them strengthen it?' He holds that this is particularly characteristic of the intellect: its property is knowledge of universals through the mediation of knowledge of particulars. Whoever denies it denies the characteristic of the human intellect. The syllogisms that the philosophers and logicians use, all of which they make universal, only rely upon examples; they depend upon the qualities of observed things, then they infer the absent from the present by the common shared aspect that they make universal. Ibn Taymiyya says: if this is not correct, then no one on earth would have universal knowledge that includes both what he has witnessed and what is absent from him, even his saying 'Bread satisfies... water quenches' and so on. For he has only sensed particular things; whence does he know that the absent is like the present except by this method? And a person may deny something until he sees one of its kind, then he affirms the kind and thereby gains a universal ruling.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's objections to the Aristotelian syllogism and his defense of analogical reasoning (tamthil) as equivalent to categorical syllogism (shumul). The author begins by illustrating with the example of khamr and intoxicants: if the premises 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden' are self-evident, the conclusion is immediately obvious without any formal syllogistic arrangement. However, Ibn Taymiyya notes that such premises are rarely self-evident; they are usually established by revelation or consensus, so the syllogism is not the primary means of knowledge. He then argues that many universal propositions are known directly through sensory perception of particulars: we observe individual humans and inductively conclude that all humans are sentient, mobile, and rational. If only syllogism could yield universal knowledge, we would face an infinite regress or circularity, as every syllogism requires a universal premise. Therefore, some universal premises must be known without syllogism. He then challenges the logicians' claim that analogy (tamthil) is only probable while categorical syllogism yields certainty, insisting that both can produce certainty or probability depending on the evidence. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that categorical syllogism and analogical reasoning are fundamentally identical: the universal in syllogism is simply the common attribute that links the original and branch in analogy. He traces the origin of syllogism back to analogy based on experience: empirical observation gives particular instances, then the intellect abstracts a universal commonality. Without this method, even everyday knowledge like 'bread satisfies hunger' would be impossible, as we only experience particular breads. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya upholds the primacy of particulars and the unity of all forms of inference, rejecting the special epistemological status granted to Aristotelian syllogism.

Key Terms

كلي
جزئي

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Identification of Analogy and Categorical Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya's argument for the identity of analogical inference and categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare Ibn Taymiyya's view of qiyas al-tamthil and qiyas al-shumul and explain why he considers them identical.

Core Idea
The universal in categorical syllogism is the same as the shared attribute in analogical inference.

In the latter part of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya reasserts a radical claim: analogical inference (qiyas al-tamthil) and categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul) are one and the same. He argues that the universal that binds all individuals in a categorical syllogism is exactly the common attribute that links the original case (asl) and the branch (far') in legal analogy. Moreover, he claims that categorical syllogism actually originates from analogical reasoning based on experience. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that qiyas al-tamthil and qiyas al-shumul are one. Find the sentence: 'فإن قياس التمثيل وقياس الشمول واحد'.
2
Extract the reasoning
He says: qiyas al-shumul means the inclusion of individuals under a general ruling; qiyas al-tamthil means two things sharing a ruling. The basis (manat) of the analogy is the universal in the syllogism. Therefore, they are identical.
3
Understand the epistemological priority
He claims that knowledge of universals comes from apprehending shared attributes among particulars through experience. The intellect abstracts the universal from observed instances. Thus, all universal propositions ultimately depend on analogical abstraction.
4
Reflect on the implication
If this is true, then the logicians' distinction between certain categorical syllogism and probable analogy collapses; both can be certain or probable depending on evidence.

كل فرد من أفراد المسكر خمر ، كان علمنا بالبديهة أن هذه الأفراد حرام من أسهل الأشياء . ويرى أبن تيمية أن ذلك انما يخفى على أكثر الناس ، لكون المقدمات غير بديهية ، بل تكون مبينة بغيرها ، كما في المثال The two self-evident premises: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden', even if these are not self-evident, but the purpose is illustration so that the ruling of other propositions may be known by the example. So if we suppose that we know by intuition that every individual of the individuals of khamr is forbidden, and we know by intuition that every individual of the individuals of intoxicant is khamr, then our knowledge by intuition that these individuals are forbidden is among the easiest of things. Their logic 'wastes time, increases rambling, and wearies the minds'. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that among universal propositions, there are those that can be known without the mediation of syllogism. If by syllogism, and the syllogism must include a universal proposition, then it would follow that the universal is not known except by the universal. He holds that this is particularly characteristic of the intellect: its property is knowledge of universals through the mediation of knowledge of particulars. Whoever denies it denies the characteristic of the human intellect. Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's objections to the Aristotelian syllogism and his defense of analogical reasoning (tamthil) as equivalent to categorical syllogism (shumul). The author begins by illustrating with the example of khamr and intoxicants: if the premises 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden' are self-evident, the conclusion is immediately obvious without any formal syllogistic arrangement. However, Ibn Taymiyya notes that such premises are rarely self-evident; they are usually established by revelation or consensus, so the syllogism is not the primary means of knowledge. He then argues that many universal propositions are known directly through sensory perception of particulars: we observe individual humans and inductively conclude that all humans are sentient, mobile, and rational. If only syllogism could yield universal knowledge, we would face an infinite regress or circularity, as every syllogism requires a universal premise. Therefore, some universal premises must be known without syllogism. He then challenges the logicians' claim that analogy (tamthil) is only probable while categorical syllogism yields certainty, insisting that both can produce certainty or probability depending on the evidence. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that categorical syllogism and analogical reasoning are fundamentally identical: the universal in syllogism is simply the common attribute that links the original and branch in analogy. He traces the origin of syllogism back to analogy based on experience: empirical observation gives particular instances, then the intellect abstracts a universal commonality. Without this method, even everyday knowledge like 'bread satisfies hunger' would be impossible, as we only experience particular breads. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya upholds the primacy of particulars and the unity of all forms of inference, rejecting the special epistemological status granted to Aristotelian syllogism.

قياس التمثيل

A) The universal is more certain than the shared attribute.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya collapses the distinction between analogy and categorical syllogism, arguing that all universal inference is ultimately analogical and grounded in experience.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Ibn Taymiyya's Reduction of Conditional and Disjunctive Syllogisms to Analogical Reasoning
Author presents Ibn Taymiyya's argument that all syllogisms reduce to analogical or universal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reduction of conditional and disjunctive syllogisms to analogical reasoning and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya claims all syllogisms reduce to intuitive knowledge or analogical reasoning.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a powerful critique against the formal syllogistic logic of the Aristotelians. He argues that conditional and disjunctive syllogisms are not irreducible tools of reasoning; rather, they can be reduced either to analogical reasoning (tamthil) or are known intuitively. This lesson walks you through his argument, using concrete Islamic examples, and asks you to assess whether his reduction successfully eliminates the need for formal logical structures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Locate the initial assertion in the passage: that only the conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani) can be reduced to analogical reasoning, while the disjunctive conditional cannot. This is the position Ibn Taymiyya refutes.
The target claim is found at the start of the passage.
2
Trace the reduction of conditional syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya shows that a conditional 'If A then B' can be transformed into a universal categorical 'Every A is B' or an implication relation. He gives the fiqh example: 'If the prayer is valid, then the prayer is pure' reduces to 'Every prayer is pure'. This works because the middle term in the conditional corresponds to the Muslim method of inference (malzūm/lāzim).
Note how the conditional's antecedent becomes the subject, and the consequent becomes the predicate of a universal proposition.
3
Examine the handling of disjunctive syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya argues that disjunctive 'either A or B' is known intuitively (bi-al-badīha). The even/odd example shows that from knowing a particular is even, we immediately know it is not odd, without needing the universal 'Every even is not odd'. Thus, such syllogisms are not logically necessary.
This step challenges the need for formal disjunctive syllogisms in reasoning.
4
Assess the overall reduction strategy
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's reduction succeeds. Does converting conditionals to categoricals preserve logical force? Is his reliance on intuition sufficient to replace formal logic? Think about counterarguments: modus ponens and modus tollens might lose generality when reduced to analogical reasoning.
This evaluation step is crucial for understanding the limits of his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقية هو الذي يمكن رده الى التمثيل ، وهو القياس الإقتراني ، بينما لا يمكن رد قياس التقسيم وهو الشرطي المنفصل . وذلك لأن الحد الأوسط في الإقتراني يمكن أن نجعله الجامع المشترك في التمثيل بخلاف القياس الاستثنائي في نوعيه . ويرد ابن تيمية بأنه يمكن رد القياس الشرطي الإتصالي والإنفصالي إلى القياس التمثيلي والقياس الشمولي . إننا إذا قلنا في قياس التلازم : هذا مستلزم لهذا ، حيث وجد وجد ، فإن هذه قضية كلية يمكن استعمالها على وجه التمثيل وعلى وجه الشمول . بأن يقاس بعض أفرادها ببعض . وأن نجعل القدر المشترك مناط الحكم واذا قلنا في قياس التقسيم « هذا إما كذا وإما كذا » فهذه أيضاً قضية كلية يمكن أن تبين بصيغة التمثيل وبصيغة الشمول . إن الأحكام الثابتة بصيغة العموم يمكن أن نستعمل فيها قياس التمثيل ؛ بأن يقاس بعض أفرادها ببعض . وأن نجعل المعنى المشترك هو مناط الحكم . ويرى ابن تيمية أن كل قياس في العالم يمكن رده إلى القياس الإقتراني فإذا قلنا مثلاً بصيغة الشرط : إن كانت الصلاة صحيحة ، فالمصلي متطهر أمكن ردها الى : كل مصل فهو متطهر : أو الصلاة مستلزمة للطهارة . وإذا كان الحد الأوسط في القضية الشرطية يقرر أن استثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي ، واستثناء نقيض التالي ، ينتج نقيض المقدم ، فإن هذا يساوي تماماً طريق الإستدلال عند المسلمين : إذا وجد الملزوم وجد اللازم وإذا انتفى اللازم ، انتفى الملزوم ، فالمقدم هو الملزوم ، والتالي هو اللازم وهكذا كل شرط وجزاء . فالشرط ملزوم . والجزاء لازم له . وكذلك الأمر في القياس الشرطي المنفصل يمكن رده إلى القياس الإقتراني . بل إن الشرطي المنفصل ، أو التقسيم أو الترديد يعلم بالبديهة . فاذا قلنا « هذا إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً ولا يجتمع هذا وهذا معاً » « وهو شفع فلا يكون وتراً » أو « هو وتر فلا يكون شفعاً . إننا نصل إلى كل هذا بالبديهة ، وتصور أفرادها أبين وأظهر من تصور كليتها ، فلسنا أبداً في حاجة إلى نظمها في قياس منطقي . يقول ابن تيمية ، « إن أي شيء علم أنه شفع ، علم أنه ليس بوتر ، بدون أن يوزن بأمر كلي عنده ، ولا يقاس على نظيره فلا يحتاج أن يقال « وكل شفع فليس بوتر ، وكل وتر فليس بشفع » فمفردات هذه القضية تعلم بدون القضية الكلية ونصل اليها أيضاً بدون التمثيل(۱) .
The logical [syllogism] that can be reduced to analogical reasoning is the conjunctive syllogism, while the division syllogism, which is the disjunctive conditional, cannot be reduced. This is because the middle term in the conjunctive can be made the common unifier in analogical reasoning, unlike the exceptional syllogism in its two types. Ibn Taymiyya responds that the conditional conjunctive and disjunctive syllogisms can be reduced to analogical and universal reasoning. We say in the syllogism of implication: 'This implies that, wherever it exists it exists' – this is a universal proposition that can be used in an analogical manner and in a universal manner, by measuring some of its instances by others, and making the common feature the basis of the judgment. And when we say in the syllogism of division: 'This is either such or such' – this too is a universal proposition that can be expressed in analogical form and universal form. Indeed, the judgments established in universal form can use analogical reasoning, by measuring some individuals by others, and making the common meaning the basis of the judgment. Ibn Taymiyya holds that every syllogism in the world can be reduced to the conjunctive syllogism. For example, if we say in conditional form: 'If the prayer is valid, then the prayer is pure' – it can be reduced to: 'Every prayer is pure' or 'Prayer necessitates purity'. And since the middle term in the conditional proposition establishes that positing the antecedent yields the consequent, and positing the negation of the consequent yields the negation of the antecedent, this is exactly the method of inference among Muslims: if the implied exists, the implication exists; if the implication is absent, the implied is absent. So the antecedent is the implied, and the consequent is the implication. And so it is with every condition and consequence: the condition is the implied, and the consequence is the implication. Similarly, the disjunctive conditional syllogism can be reduced to the conjunctive syllogism. Moreover, the disjunctive conditional, or division or disjunction, is known intuitively. If we say: 'This is either even or odd, and these two cannot coexist' – and it is even, so it is not odd; or it is odd, so it is not even. We arrive at all this intuitively, and the conception of its individuals is clearer and more evident than the conception of its universal. We are never in need of framing it in a logical syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'Anything known to be even is known not to be odd, without being weighed by a universal proposition, nor measured by its counterpart. There is no need to say: "Every even is not odd, and every odd is not even." The individuals of this proposition are known without the universal proposition, and we arrive at it also without analogical reasoning.'
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange: the author first asserts that only the conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyās al-iqṭirānī) can be reduced to analogical reasoning (tamthīl), and the division syllogism (qiyās al-taqsīm) – i.e., the disjunctive conditional – cannot be reduced. Ibn Taymiyya then refutes this claim. He argues that both types of conditional syllogisms can be reduced to tamthīl. He starts with the syllogism of implication (qiyās al-talāzum), where a universal proposition like 'whenever this exists, that exists' can be applied analogically by comparing individual instances. Similarly, the disjunctive 'either A or B' is a universal proposition that can be used analogically. He then extends the reduction to the conjunctive categorical syllogism: every syllogism reduces to iqṭirānī. He provides a fiqh example: the conditional 'If prayer is valid, then the prayer is pure' reduces to the categorical 'Every prayer is pure'. This works because the middle term in the conditional syllogism corresponds to the Muslim method of inference (istidlāl) where the antecedent is the implied (malzūm) and the consequent is the implication (lāzim). Thus, the conditional syllogism is just a variant of categorical reasoning. He then tackles the disjunctive syllogism, claiming it is known intuitively (bi-al-badīha). The example of 'even or odd' shows that knowing a particular is even suffices to know it is not odd, without needing a universal premise. He quotes himself to emphasize that individuals are known directly without universal propositions or analogical comparison. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya deconstructs the necessity of formal syllogistic logic by showing that all logical forms reduce to intuitive knowledge of particulars or analogical reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Aristotelian Logic
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of syllogistic logic and call for experiential method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's main objections to Aristotelian syllogistic logic and identify his proposed empirical alternative.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya replaces syllogistic logic with empirical experience as the only reliable path to knowledge.

This passage presents the core of Ibn Taymiyya's attack on Aristotelian logic. He argues that syllogistic reasoning is artificial and disconnected from reality, while experience (tajriba), especially as practiced by physicians, yields certain knowledge. We will walk through his critique and his alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological starting point
Notice how he prioritizes particulars ('al-mu'ayyanat') over universals, rejecting the need for universal premises like 'every even is not odd' when particulars are known directly.
2
Compare philosophical syllogism with medical experience
Examine his comparison: medical experiments are reliable because they involve multiple testers, abundant materials, and observable results, unlike the abstract claims of philosophers.
This illustrates his empirical criterion for knowledge.
3
Understand the civilizational argument
He sees Greek logic as a foreign methodology that, if mixed with Islamic sciences, will undermine Islamic civilization from within. Identify how he frames this as a clash of civilizations.
He calls Greek logic a 'pickaxe' demolishing Islamic foundations.
4
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's positive proposal
He advocates for experience (tajriba) as the primary method, expanding inference beyond syllogism, induction, and analogy, and focusing on accidents (a'rad) rather than essences (mahiyya).
He also emphasizes quantity (kamm) over quality (kayf) and rejects pure speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأمر كلي عنده ، ولا يقاس على نظيره فلا يحتاج أن يقال « وكل شفع فليس بوتر ، وكل وتر فليس بشفع » فمفردات هذه القضية تعلم بدون القضية الكلية ونصل اليها أيضاً بدون التمثيل(۱) .
إن ابن تيمية يؤمن بالمعينات ، بالجزئيات ، ويرى أن المنطق القياسي لا يصل إلى شيء واقعي . وينتهي إلى القول بأن التجربة وحدها هي أقرب إلى الحقيقة مما ينتجه الفلاسفة بقياس . وتجربة الأطباء هي أسد التجارب يقول « إن ما يدعى في تجارب الاطباء من الأدوية أقرب إلى التسليم من تجاربهم الرصدية وذلك لاشتراك كثير من الأطباء في تجربة الدواء الواحد ، وأيضاً فلكثرة وجود ما يدعى فيه التجربة من النبات ، وتواطؤ المجربين له وليس كذلك ما يدعونه من فلسفتهم : فإنا رأينا تجارب الأطباء على غير منوالهم . وعلمنا صدقها كثيراً . . . والأطباء في تجاربهم أسد حالاً منهم ، لأن القوم إنما يذكرون دواءاً موجوداً يمكن تجربته كثيراً لوجوده ، ولكثرة المحتاجين إليه ممن يصيبه مما يقال : إن ذلك الدواء يؤثر فيه شفاء(۲) . وليس هناك في الحقيقة من تكلم - فيما قبل العصور الحديثة - بما تكلم به ابن تيمية . لقد وصل حقاً إلى أوج الدرج في فلسفة المنهج التجريبي ، بنقده للمنطق اليوناني القياسي وبدعوته إلى المنطق الإسلامي التجريبي ، وعبر عن روح الحضارة الإسلامية الحقة في عصر الإنهيار الحضاري الإسلامي الذي عاش فيه .
لقد تنبه ابن تيمية - إلى ما تنبه اليه الاقدمون من نظار المسلمين من اختلاف الطريقين - طريق اليونان وطريق المسلمين - في العلم ، وأن كل طريق من هذين الطريقين قد أقام حضارة مباينة للحضارة الأخرى . وأن كل طريق منهما كان منهج حضارة معينة ، فلما قامت دعوة بعض المسلمين إلى استخدام هذا المنطق ومزجه بعلوم المسلمين ، صرخ ابن تيمية صرخته ، وقد أدرك بعمق بالغ أن أعظم معول ينقض في أساس الحضارة الإسلامية ، هو معول المنطق اليوناني . إنه سيقوضها باسم المنهج ، وسينتج قضايا عامة في كل النطاقات تمزق الأحكام العامة للمسلمين ، فتتبع قواعد هذا المنطق وقام بتطبيقها ، فلم ينتج له سوى آراء بعيدة كل البعد عن ما هو إسلامي فأعلن أن منطق أرسطو إنما يستند على ميتافيزيقاه وإلهياته ، وهذه الإلهيات مخالفة لإلهيات المسلمين ، ومن هنا نشأت استحالة تطبيق مباحث المنطق الأرسططاليسي على مباحث المسلمين الإلهية . أما تطبيق مباحث المنطق الأرسططاليسي على مختلف العلوم ، فقد رأى ابن تيمية ما في مباحث هذا المنطق من تكلف وتصنع ، وتضييق طرق الذهن في نطاق معين هي « طرق صعبة طويلة ، وعبارات متكلفة حائلة » وليس لها من نتيجة سوى إضاعة الوقت وإتعاب الأذهان . إن اتجاه الإسلام - كمنهج - أن يفي بالحاجات الإنسانية ، والحاجة الإنسانية لا تستقر ولا تسكن ، إنها في تغير مستمر فلا تتوقف فقط أمام « ماهيات جامدة » إن حقائق الأشياء الإنسانية والطبيعية في تغير مستمر ، فنلجأ نحن فقط إلى تحديد أعراضها وخواصها أما أن يحاول اكتناه الماهية ، فمطمع عسير . كما أن نتكلف قياساً يقتصر على مقدمتين ، فان معناه أن نحصر أنفسنا في طرق ميكانيكية . كما أن من الخطأ أن نقتصر في الإستدلال العقلي على القياس والإستقراء والتمثيل ، إننا نستطيع أن نصل إلى صور أكثر - إذا حللنا العمليات الفكرية ، ولقد نادى ابن تيمية بفكرة « الكم » أساساً لكل عملية فكرية ، ولم يؤمن بسلطان الكيف إلا نادراً ، كما أنه لم يؤمن بالنظر البحت ، بل نادى بالتجربة . إنه كان يتكلم باسم الروح الحضاري الإسلامي ، ممجداً التجربة ، معلناً أنها هي الطريق الوحيد للوصول إلى العلم سواء أكان يقينياً أو ظنياً . وسننتقل الآن الى مناقشة الجانب الإنشائي لنقد ابن تيمية لمباحث الإستدلال الارسططاليسي .
With a universal matter [in his view], and it is not measured by its counterpart, so there is no need to say 'Every even is not odd, and every odd is not even,' because the singulars of this proposition are known without the universal proposition, and we also reach them without analogy(1).
Ibn Taymiyya believes in particulars, in individual things, and sees that syllogistic logic does not reach anything real. He concludes that experience alone is closer to truth than what philosophers produce by syllogism. The experience of physicians is the most reliable of experiences. He says: 'What is claimed in the experiences of physicians regarding medicines is closer to acceptance than their observational experiences, because of the participation of many physicians in testing a single medicine, and also because of the abundance of what is claimed to be tested from plants, and the consensus of those who test it. This is not the case with what they claim from their philosophy: for we have seen the experiences of physicians on a different pattern, and we have known their truth often... And physicians in their experiences are more correct than them, because the people only mention a medicine that exists and can be tested many times due to its existence, and due to the abundance of those in need of it who are afflicted with that which it is said that medicine cures(2). And there is in reality no one - before modern times - who spoke as Ibn Taymiyya spoke. He truly reached the apex of the philosophy of the experimental method, with his critique of Greek syllogistic logic and his call for Islamic experimental logic, and he expressed the spirit of true Islamic civilization in the era of Islamic civilizational decline in which he lived.
Ibn Taymiyya recognized - what earlier Muslim thinkers recognized - the difference between the two paths: the path of the Greeks and the path of the Muslims in knowledge, and that each of these two paths established a civilization different from the other, and that each path was the method of a particular civilization. When the call arose among some Muslims to use this logic and mix it with Islamic sciences, Ibn Taymiyya cried out his cry, and he realized with profound depth that the greatest pickaxe that demolishes the foundation of Islamic civilization is the pickaxe of Greek logic. It will undermine it in the name of method, and it will produce universal propositions in all domains that tear apart the general rulings of Muslims. So he followed the rules of this logic and applied them, and it produced nothing for him but opinions far removed from what is Islamic. He declared that Aristotle's logic is based on his metaphysics and theology, and this theology contradicts Muslim theology, and from this arose the impossibility of applying the topics of Aristotelian logic to the theological topics of Muslims. As for applying the topics of Aristotelian logic to various sciences, Ibn Taymiyya saw in the topics of this logic affectation and artificiality, and a narrowing of the mind's paths into a specific scope; they are 'difficult lengthy paths, and affected obstructive phrases' with no result other than wasting time and tiring minds. Indeed, the direction of Islam - as a method - is to fulfill human needs, and human need does not settle nor rest; it is in continuous change, so it does not stop only before 'static essences'. Indeed, the realities of human and natural things are in continuous change, so we only resort to determining their accidents and properties. As for attempting to grasp the essence, it is a difficult ambition. Also, that we impose upon ourselves a syllogism that is limited to two premises, its meaning is to confine ourselves to mechanical methods. And it is also a mistake to limit intellectual inference to syllogism, induction, and analogy. We can reach more forms - if we analyze intellectual operations. And Ibn Taymiyya called for the idea of 'quantity' as the basis for every intellectual operation, and he believed in the authority of quality only rarely. He did not believe in pure speculation, but called for experience. He spoke in the name of the Islamic civilizational spirit, glorifying experience, declaring that it is the only path to attaining knowledge, whether certain or probable. And we will now move to discuss the constructive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the topics of Aristotelian inference.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's comprehensive critique of Aristotelian logic and his advocacy for an empirical, experience-based methodology. The author begins by noting Ibn Taymiyya's focus on particulars ('al-mu'ayyanat') rather than universals, rejecting the claim that syllogistic logic can yield real knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that experience (tajriba) alone is closer to truth than the qiyas (syllogism) of philosophers. He uses the example of medical experiments: physicians collectively testing a medicine, due to the abundance of the medicine and the frequency of its application, produce reliable knowledge. This contrasts with the abstract, unverifiable claims of Greek philosophy. The author then highlights Ibn Taymiyya's historical significance as a precursor to modern experimental philosophy, calling him the zenith of the empirical method in Islamic civilization.

Ibn Taymiyya recognized the fundamental difference between the Greek and Islamic paths to knowledge—each arising from distinct civilizational frameworks. He vehemently opposed the attempt to graft Greek logic onto Islamic sciences, seeing it as a 'pickaxe' that would demolish Islamic civilization from within. He argued that Aristotle's logic is inseparable from his metaphysics, which contradicts Islamic theology (ilahiyyat). Thus, applying Aristotelian logic to Islamic theological matters is impossible. Even for other sciences, Ibn Taymiyya found Aristotelian logic to be artificial, narrowing the mind's natural pathways with cumbersome, lengthy methods that waste time.

Moreover, Ibn Taymiyya critiqued the limitation of reasoning to syllogism, induction, and analogy. He believed that by analyzing intellectual operations, we can develop more forms of inference. He championed 'quantity' (al-kamm) as the basis of thought, downplaying 'quality' (al-kayf), and rejected pure speculation (al-nazar al-baht) in favor of experience. For him, experience is the only path to knowledge, whether certain (yaqini) or probable (zanni). The passage concludes by noting that the author will next discuss the constructive side of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Aristotelian Syllogism
Author explains Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the syllogism's reliance on universal premises and why he considers it artificial.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the syllogism as unnatural and offers a direct particular-to-particular implication instead.

Ibn Taymiyya did not reject reasoning altogether; he specifically criticized the Aristotelian syllogism for being convoluted and contrary to human innate disposition (fitra). He argued that the syllogism requires first verifying a universal premise by examining all its particulars, which is impractical. His alternative is based on necessary implication (istilzam) between particulars, which he considers more natural and precise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Locate in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya attacks the Aristotelian syllogism (qiyas mantiqi). Note his description of it as 'complicated, affected way far from innate disposition'.
The passage contrasts the syllogism with the 'proof of signs'.
2
Extract the objection
Ibn Taymiyya says the syllogism requires the mind to first realize a universal proposition by checking all its particular individuals, then use that universal in the syllogism. This is tedious and indirect.
3
Understand the assumed alternative
Contrast this with his preferred method: the implication of a particular for a particular (istilzam), which does not need a universal premise.
This sets up the next lesson on istilzam.

بطلوع الشمس ، يوجب العلم بوجود النهار . ومعجزات محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم آية على نبوته . فنفس العلم بثبوت تلك المعجزات ، يوجب العلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم . والعلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يوجب أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره ، لأن هذه المعجزات خاصة به وحده . وكذلك آيات الله، نفس العلم بها ، يوجب العلم بذاته - وهذا العلم لا يوجب علماً مشتركاً بين الله وغيره . ويرى ابن تيمية أن العلم يكون هذا مستلزماً لهذا ، هو جهة الدليل ، ولا بد أن يكون كل دليل في الوجود مستلزماً للمدلول . والآية - وهي العلم باستلزام المعين للمعين المطلوب - أدق وأقرب إلى الفطرة من القياس المنطقي الذي ينتقل فيه العقل من حكم كلي عام إلى أحكام جزئية . ولا بد للفكر أن يستخدم لتحقيق هذه القضية الكلية تحقيق أفرادها المعينة أي الجزئية ، وأن يشمل كل فرد من هذه الأفراد ما يشمل الحكم الكلي . يفعل هذا المناطقة لتحقيق صحة القضية الكلية ، ثم يستخدم بعد ذلك في القياس . ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا طريق معقد متكلف بعيد عن الفطرة ، إن طريق الفكر الحقيقي عند ابن تيمية - كما هو عند المتكلمين - هو إستلزام الدليل للمدلول ، هو إستلزام معين لمعين . فيلزم من وجود الخاص ، وجود الخاص كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان ، وجود هذا الإنسان الآخر ، ومن وجود هذا الإنسان وجود إنسان ثالث . أي يستدل بالمعين على المعين . ولكن إذا كان هناك علم كلي ، فانه ينشأ من العلم الجزئي ، فيلزم من وجود الخاص ، وجود العام كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان ، وجود الإنسان . ومن وجود هذا الإنسان ، وجود الإنسانية والحيوانية . ولكن ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن المطلق لا يوجد بما هو مطلق إلا في الأذهان ، ونفس هذا المطلق لا يوصل إلى معرفة أصلاً « فإذا علم الإنسان وجود إنسان مطلق وحيوان مطلق ، لم يكن عالماً بنفس العين . كذلك إذا علم واجباً مطلقاً وفاعلاً مطلقاً وغنياً مطلقاً لم يكن عالماً بنفس رب العالمين ، وعلى هذا الأساس لا يمكن التوصل إلى معرفة الله ، وما يختص به عن غيره » فيرى ابن تيمية حينئذ أن نلجأ إلى دليل الآيات « فآياته تستلزم عينه التي يمنع تصورها من وقوع الشركة فيها . وكل ما سواه دليل على عينه وآية له ـ فإنه ملزوم لعينه ، فانه دليل على لازمه . ويمتنع تحقق شيء من الممكنات إلا مع تحقق عينه ، فكلها لازمة لنفسه دليل عليه آية له » وإذا كانت الآية تختلف إختلافاً جوهرياً عن القياس الأرسططاليسي فانها تخالف أيضاً التمثيل الأرسططاليسي . يقول ابن تيمية إن دليل الآية هو « إستدلال بجزئي على جزئي لتلازمهما وليس ذلك من قياس التمثيل» ولا يعلل ابن تيمية الخلاف بينهما بأكثر من ذلك . أما توضيح هذا الخلاف فاني أوضحه فيما يأتي : إن دليل الآية يشبه في الحقيقة مسلك الدوران في مباحث العلة الأصولية ، أي دوران المقدم أو العلة مع التالي أو المعلول وجوداً وعدماً . وهو ما يسميه المحدثون قانون التلازم في الوقوع وفي التخلف . وهذا ما يوضح لنا الإختلاف الشديد بين طريقي الآية والتمثيل الأرسططاليسي .
With the rising of the sun, knowledge of the existence of daytime occurs. And the miracles of Muhammad, peace be upon him, are a sign of his prophethood. So the very knowledge of the existence of those miracles necessitates knowledge of the existence of Muhammad's prophethood. And knowledge of the existence of Muhammad's prophethood does not necessitate a universal matter common between him and others, because these miracles are specific to him alone. Likewise, the signs of God, the very knowledge of them necessitates knowledge of His essence — and this knowledge does not necessitate knowledge common between God and others. Ibn Taymiyya holds that knowledge being such that it necessitates this is the direction of the proof, and it is necessary that every proof in existence be necessitating of the proved. And the sign — which is knowledge of the implication of the particular for the particular required — is more precise and closer to innate disposition than the logical syllogism in which the mind moves from a general universal judgment to particular judgments. The mind must, to realize this universal proposition, realize its particular individuals, i.e., the particulars, and that every individual of these individuals includes what the universal judgment includes. The logicians do this to verify the soundness of the universal proposition, then use it afterwards in the syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is a complicated, affected way far from innate disposition. The true path of thought for Ibn Taymiyya — as it is for the theologians — is the implication of the proof for the proved, it is the implication of a particular for a particular. So from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the particular, just as from the existence of this human follows the existence of that other human, and from the existence of this human follows the existence of a third human. That is, one infer from the particular to the particular. But if there is universal knowledge, it arises from particular knowledge, so from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the universal, just as from the existence of this human follows the existence of human, and from the existence of this human follows the existence of humanity and animality. But we must note that the absolute does not exist as absolute except in minds, and this very absolute does not lead to knowledge at all. 'If a person knows the existence of absolute human and absolute animal, he is not knowing the essence itself. Likewise, if he knows absolute necessary being, absolute agent, absolute rich, he is not knowing the essence of the Lord of the worlds, and on this basis it is not possible to arrive at knowledge of God and what distinguishes Him from others.' So Ibn Taymiyya holds then that we must resort to the proof of signs: 'For His signs necessitate His essence, the conception of which prevents the occurrence of sharing in it. And everything other than Him is a proof of His essence and a sign for Him — for it is implied by His essence, so it is a proof of its necessary concomitant. And it is impossible for any possible thing to be realized except with the realization of His essence, so all are necessary concomitants of Himself, a proof of Him, a sign for Him.' And if the sign differs essentially from the Aristotelian syllogism, it also differs from the Aristotelian analogy. Ibn Taymiyya says that the proof of the sign is 'inference from a particular to a particular due to their mutual implication, and that is not from the analogical syllogism.' And Ibn Taymiyya does not explain the difference between them more than that. As for clarifying this difference, I clarify it as follows: The proof of the sign resembles in reality the path of rotation in the investigations of the cause in usul, i.e., the rotation of the antecedent or cause with the consequent or effect in existence and nonexistence. And that is what the later scholars call the law of concomitance in occurrence and in co-absence. And this clarifies for us the severe difference between the two paths of the sign and the Aristotelian analogy.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic and his alternative method of reasoning known as 'dil al-ayah' (proof of signs). The author begins by illustrating the concept with examples: the rising of the sun necessarily implies daytime, and the miracles of Muhammad necessarily imply his prophethood. Crucially, these implications are particular-to-particular: the knowledge of a specific miracle leads directly to knowledge of a specific prophethood, not to a universal property shared with others. Similarly, knowledge of God's signs leads to knowledge of His essence, not to a common attribute. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this type of implication (istilzam) is the true direction of proof (jiha al-dalil). He contrasts this with the Aristotelian syllogism, which moves from a universal judgment to particular judgments. The syllogism requires first verifying the universal proposition by examining all its particulars, which is cumbersome and artificial. Ibn Taymiyya's preferred method is the implication of a particular for a particular: from the existence of one particular, the existence of another particular follows by necessity. He gives examples like inferring the existence of one human from another, or inferring the existence of a third human from two given humans. Even universal knowledge, he says, arises from particular knowledge; from the existence of a particular human we infer the universal 'human' or 'animality'. However, Ibn Taymiyya warns that the absolute universal exists only in the mind and cannot lead to knowledge of specific essences. For example, knowing 'absolute human' does not give knowledge of a particular human being. This is especially important for knowledge of God: knowing 'absolute necessary being' does not give knowledge of the specific Lord of the worlds. Therefore, we must use the proof of signs: every created thing is a sign that necessitates God's essence, because any possible thing cannot exist without His essence. This proof of signs is fundamentally different from both Aristotelian syllogism (qiyas) and analogical reasoning (tamthil). The author then clarifies that this proof of signs resembles the 'maslak al-dawaran' (path of rotation) in usul al-fiqh, where the cause or antecedent rotates in existence and nonexistence with the effect or consequent. This is the law of concomitance. Thus, the passage firmly establishes Ibn Taymiyya's critique and offers a positive alternative grounded in necessary implication between particulars.

Key Terms
قياس منطقي
استلزام
آية
فطرة

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is a key weakness of the Aristotelian syllogism?
A) It moves from particular to particular, which is too specific.
B) It requires verifying a universal premise by examining all its particulars, which is complicated.
C) It relies on analogical reasoning, which is invalid.
D) It cannot be used to prove anything about God.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the Aristotelian syllogism for its cumbersome universal verification and proposes a more direct particular-to-particular inference as the natural method of reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a theological argument for God's existence that uses the proof of signs rather than a syllogism. Explain how it moves from particular to particular.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Proof of Signs (Dil al-Ayah) as an Alternative Method
Author explains Ibn Taymiyya's proof of signs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a simple proof of signs from a particular sign to a particular thing and distinguish it from syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
The proof of signs infers directly from one particular to another joined by necessary implication.

Ibn Taymiyya's positive alternative to the syllogism is the 'proof of signs' (dil al-ayah). This method is based on the idea that certain things are necessarily connected, so knowing one directly leads to knowing the other. It is particular-to-particular, not universal-to-particular. The passage illustrates this with examples like the rising sun implying daytime and Muhammad's miracles implying his prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sign (ayah) and the signified (madalul)
In the passage, find examples: rising sun (sign) and daytime (signified); miracles of Muhammad (sign) and his prophethood (signified).
2
Establish the necessary connection (istilzam)
Note that the connection is not arbitrary but necessary: if the sign exists, the signified must exist. This is istilzam.
Istilzam is the core concept.
3
Draw the inference directly
The inference is immediate: from the existence of the particular sign, the existence of the particular signified follows, without any middle term or universal premise.
This is what makes it different from a syllogism.

The Syllogism

Distinguishing the Proof of Signs from Analogical Reasoning (Tamthil)
Author distinguishes proof of signs from analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between Ibn Taymiyya's proof of signs and Aristotelian analogy (tamthil) based on the nature of the inference and its connection to usul al-fiqh's rotation method.

Core Idea
The proof of signs is a direct particular-to-particular implication, while analogy uses a shared attribute as a middle term.

Ibn Taymiyya explicitly separates his proof of signs from Aristotelian analogical reasoning (tamthil). The passage states that the proof of signs is 'inference from a particular to a particular due to their mutual implication,' not analogical reasoning. The author then clarifies that this method resembles the 'path of rotation' (maslak al-dawaran) in usul al-fiqh, where a cause and effect rotate together. This lesson will sharpen your ability to distinguish these two inferential methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of distinction
Find where the passage says the proof of signs is different from tamthil. The author quotes Ibn Taymiyya: 'istidlal bi-juz'i 'ala juz'i li-talazumihima wa laysa dhalika min qiyas al-tamthil'.
This is the key sentence.
2
Understand tamthil (analogy)
Recognize that tamthil moves from one particular to another via a shared universal attribute (the 'illa'). For example, wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; other intoxicants are analogically forbidden.
Tamthil requires a common property.
3
Contrast with proof of signs
In proof of signs, the connection is direct and necessary without any shared attribute. The sun and daytime are connected by causation, not by a common middle term.
The connection is essential, not analogical.
4
Connect to dawaran in usul
The author says proof of signs resembles maslak al-dawaran: the cause rotates with the effect. For example, if a ruling is always present when a specific attribute is present, and absent when it is absent, then that attribute is the 'illa.
This is a particular-to-particular inference of necessity.

بطلوع الشمس ، يوجب العلم بوجود النهار . ومعجزات محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم آية على نبوته . فنفس العلم بثبوت تلك المعجزات ، يوجب العلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم . والعلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يوجب أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره ، لأن هذه المعجزات خاصة به وحده . وكذلك آيات الله، نفس العلم بها ، يوجب العلم بذاته - وهذا العلم لا يوجب علماً مشتركاً بين الله وغيره . ويرى ابن تيمية أن العلم يكون هذا مستلزماً لهذا ، هو جهة الدليل ، ولا بد أن يكون كل دليل في الوجود مستلزماً للمدلول . والآية - وهي العلم باستلزام المعين للمعين المطلوب - أدق وأقرب إلى الفطرة من القياس المنطقي الذي ينتقل فيه العقل من حكم كلي عام إلى أحكام جزئية . ولا بد للفكر أن يستخدم لتحقيق هذه القضية الكلية تحقيق أفرادها المعينة أي الجزئية ، وأن يشمل كل فرد من هذه الأفراد ما يشمل الحكم الكلي . يفعل هذا المناطقة لتحقيق صحة القضية الكلية ، ثم يستخدم بعد ذلك في القياس . ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا طريق معقد متكلف بعيد عن الفطرة ، إن طريق الفكر الحقيقي عند ابن تيمية - كما هو عند المتكلمين - هو إستلزام الدليل للمدلول ، هو إستلزام معين لمعين . فيلزم من وجود الخاص ، وجود الخاص كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان ، وجود هذا الإنسان الآخر ، ومن وجود هذا الإنسان وجود إنسان ثالث . أي يستدل بالمعين على المعين . ولكن إذا كان هناك علم كلي ، فانه ينشأ من العلم الجزئي ، فيلزم من وجود الخاص ، وجود العام كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان ، وجود الإنسان . ومن وجود هذا الإنسان ، وجود الإنسانية والحيوانية . ولكن ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن المطلق لا يوجد بما هو مطلق إلا في الأذهان ، ونفس هذا المطلق لا يوصل إلى معرفة أصلاً « فإذا علم الإنسان وجود إنسان مطلق وحيوان مطلق ، لم يكن عالماً بنفس العين . كذلك إذا علم واجباً مطلقاً وفاعلاً مطلقاً وغنياً مطلقاً لم يكن عالماً بنفس رب العالمين ، وعلى هذا الأساس لا يمكن التوصل إلى معرفة الله ، وما يختص به عن غيره » فيرى ابن تيمية حينئذ أن نلجأ إلى دليل الآيات « فآياته تستلزم عينه التي يمنع تصورها من وقوع الشركة فيها . وكل ما سواه دليل على عينه وآية له ـ فإنه ملزوم لعينه ، فانه دليل على لازمه . ويمتنع تحقق شيء من الممكنات إلا مع تحقق عينه ، فكلها لازمة لنفسه دليل عليه آية له » وإذا كانت الآية تختلف إختلافاً جوهرياً عن القياس الأرسططاليسي فانها تخالف أيضاً التمثيل الأرسططاليسي . يقول ابن تيمية إن دليل الآية هو « إستدلال بجزئي على جزئي لتلازمهما وليس ذلك من قياس التمثيل» ولا يعلل ابن تيمية الخلاف بينهما بأكثر من ذلك . أما توضيح هذا الخلاف فاني أوضحه فيما يأتي : إن دليل الآية يشبه في الحقيقة مسلك الدوران في مباحث العلة الأصولية ، أي دوران المقدم أو العلة مع التالي أو المعلول وجوداً وعدماً . وهو ما يسميه المحدثون قانون التلازم في الوقوع وفي التخلف . وهذا ما يوضح لنا الإختلاف الشديد بين طريقي الآية والتمثيل الأرسططاليسي .
With the rising of the sun, knowledge of the existence of daytime occurs. And the miracles of Muhammad, peace be upon him, are a sign of his prophethood. So the very knowledge of the existence of those miracles necessitates knowledge of the existence of Muhammad's prophethood. And knowledge of the existence of Muhammad's prophethood does not necessitate a universal matter common between him and others, because these miracles are specific to him alone. Likewise, the signs of God, the very knowledge of them necessitates knowledge of His essence — and this knowledge does not necessitate knowledge common between God and others. Ibn Taymiyya holds that knowledge being such that it necessitates this is the direction of the proof, and it is necessary that every proof in existence be necessitating of the proved. And the sign — which is knowledge of the implication of the particular for the particular required — is more precise and closer to innate disposition than the logical syllogism in which the mind moves from a general universal judgment to particular judgments. The mind must, to realize this universal proposition, realize its particular individuals, i.e., the particulars, and that every individual of these individuals includes what the universal judgment includes. The logicians do this to verify the soundness of the universal proposition, then use it afterwards in the syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is a complicated, affected way far from innate disposition. The true path of thought for Ibn Taymiyya — as it is for the theologians — is the implication of the proof for the proved, it is the implication of a particular for a particular. So from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the particular, just as from the existence of this human follows the existence of that other human, and from the existence of this human follows the existence of a third human. That is, one infer from the particular to the particular. But if there is universal knowledge, it arises from particular knowledge, so from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the universal, just as from the existence of this human follows the existence of human, and from the existence of this human follows the existence of humanity and animality. But we must note that the absolute does not exist as absolute except in minds, and this very absolute does not lead to knowledge at all. 'If a person knows the existence of absolute human and absolute animal, he is not knowing the essence itself. Likewise, if he knows absolute necessary being, absolute agent, absolute rich, he is not knowing the essence of the Lord of the worlds, and on this basis it is not possible to arrive at knowledge of God and what distinguishes Him from others.' So Ibn Taymiyya holds then that we must resort to the proof of signs: 'For His signs necessitate His essence, the conception of which prevents the occurrence of sharing in it. And everything other than Him is a proof of His essence and a sign for Him — for it is implied by His essence, so it is a proof of its necessary concomitant. And it is impossible for any possible thing to be realized except with the realization of His essence, so all are necessary concomitants of Himself, a proof of Him, a sign for Him.' And if the sign differs essentially from the Aristotelian syllogism, it also differs from the Aristotelian analogy. Ibn Taymiyya says that the proof of the sign is 'inference from a particular to a particular due to their mutual implication, and that is not from the analogical syllogism.' And Ibn Taymiyya does not explain the difference between them more than that. As for clarifying this difference, I clarify it as follows: The proof of the sign resembles in reality the path of rotation in the investigations of the cause in usul, i.e., the rotation of the antecedent or cause with the consequent or effect in existence and nonexistence. And that is what the later scholars call the law of concomitance in occurrence and in co-absence. And this clarifies for us the severe difference between the two paths of the sign and the Aristotelian analogy.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic and his alternative method of reasoning known as 'dil al-ayah' (proof of signs). The author begins by illustrating the concept with examples: the rising of the sun necessarily implies daytime, and the miracles of Muhammad necessarily imply his prophethood. Crucially, these implications are particular-to-particular: the knowledge of a specific miracle leads directly to knowledge of a specific prophethood, not to a universal property shared with others. Similarly, knowledge of God's signs leads to knowledge of His essence, not to a common attribute. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this type of implication (istilzam) is the true direction of proof (jiha al-dalil). He contrasts this with the Aristotelian syllogism, which moves from a universal judgment to particular judgments. The syllogism requires first verifying the universal proposition by examining all its particulars, which is cumbersome and artificial. Ibn Taymiyya's preferred method is the implication of a particular for a particular: from the existence of one particular, the existence of another particular follows by necessity. He gives examples like inferring the existence of one human from another, or inferring the existence of a third human from two given humans. Even universal knowledge, he says, arises from particular knowledge; from the existence of a particular human we infer the universal 'human' or 'animality'. However, Ibn Taymiyya warns that the absolute universal exists only in the mind and cannot lead to knowledge of specific essences. For example, knowing 'absolute human' does not give knowledge of a particular human being. This is especially important for knowledge of God: knowing 'absolute necessary being' does not give knowledge of the specific Lord of the worlds. Therefore, we must use the proof of signs: every created thing is a sign that necessitates God's essence, because any possible thing cannot exist without His essence. This proof of signs is fundamentally different from both Aristotelian syllogism (qiyas) and analogical reasoning (tamthil). The author then clarifies that this proof of signs resembles the 'maslak al-dawaran' (path of rotation) in usul al-fiqh, where the cause or antecedent rotates in existence and nonexistence with the effect or consequent. This is the law of concomitance. Thus, the passage firmly establishes Ibn Taymiyya's critique and offers a positive alternative grounded in necessary implication between particulars.

Key Terms
تمثيل
دوران
تلازم
علة

Key Takeaway
The proof of signs is distinct from analogical reasoning because it infers by direct necessity between particulars, not via a shared attribute; it parallels the rotation method in usul al-fiqh.

The Syllogism

Conditional Syllogisms: Inclusion vs. Entailment
Al-Iji criticizes universal inference; the commentator addresses conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish inclusion (ishtimal) from entailment (istilzam) in conditional syllogisms and explain why conditional syllogisms are not necessarily inference from universal to particular.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms rely on entailment or inclusion, not on a universal-to-particular pattern.

In this passage, we see a debate about the nature of inference. Al-Iji critiques universal-to-universal inference, and the commentator raises an important question: Are conditional syllogisms examples of universal-to-particular inference? Through examples, he shows that conditional syllogisms involve either inclusion (particular under universal) or pure entailment, thus clarifying the fundamental relations in logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice that al-Iji tries to reduce universal-to-universal inference to analogy or Aristotelian syllogism. The commentator then asks: Are conditional syllogisms also instances of universal-to-particular inference?
2
Examine the examples
Look at the conjunctive conditional: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; but it is rising, so daytime exists.' And the disjunctive: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he will not drown; but he is not in the sea, so he will not drown.' These are standard conditional syllogisms.
3
Understand the commentator's answer
The commentator states that conditional syllogisms are not inference from universal to particular. Instead, the relation between premise and conclusion is either inclusion (ishtimal) or entailment (istilzam) without inclusion.
Inclusion means the particular falls under a universal; entailment is a necessary connection without subsumption.
4
Distinguish inclusion from entailment
Inclusion: e.g., 'All humans are mortal' includes 'Zayd is mortal'. Entailment: e.g., 'If the sun rises, day exists' — the relation is not that 'sunrise' is a particular of 'day existence'; it is a conditional connection.
5
Apply the distinction
Conjunctive conditionals have explicit entailment; disjunctive conditionals have implicit entailment. Both reduce to either inclusion or entailment, not directly to a universal-particular pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويناقش صاحب المواقف هذا الطريق «الإستدلال بالكلي على الكلي ويحاول أن يرده إما إلى التمثيل وإما إلى القياس الأرسططاليسي». ودعا هذا شارح المواقف إلى إثارة مسألة أخرى تتصل أيضاً بالإستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي. وهي: هل نعتبر القياس الإستثنائي المتصل أو المنفصل إستدلالاً بكلي على جزئي؟ فاذا قلنا: كلما كانت الشمس طالعة، كان النهار موجوداً لكنها طالعة أو لكن النهار غير موجود. أو إذا قلنا إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق ـ لكنه ليس في البحر فلا يغرق ـ أو لكنه غرق فيكون في البحر: هل نعتبر تلك الأمثلة إستدلالاً بكلي على جزئي. يرد شارح المواقف أن القياس الإستثنائي في قسميه: المتصل والمنفصل ليس استدلالاً بكلي على جزئي «ولكن الصواب أن يقال المناسبة بين الدليل والمدلول إما بالإشتمال وإما بالإستلزام الذي لا اشتمال معه، فأما صريحاً كما في الاستثناءات المتصلة وأما غير صريح كما في الاستثناءات المنفصلة: وأما الإقترانيات الشرطية فراجعة اما إلى الإستلزام أو الإشتمال».
And the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) discusses this method 'the inference from universal to universal and tries to reduce it either to analogy or to Aristotelian syllogism.' This prompted the commentator of al-Mawaqif to raise another issue also related to inference from universal to particular: Do we consider the conditional syllogism, both conjunctive and disjunctive, as inference from universal to particular? For example, if we say: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; but it is rising' or 'but daytime does not exist.' Or if we say: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he will not drown; but he is not in the sea, so he will not drown' or 'but he drowned, so he is in the sea.' Do we consider these examples as inference from universal to particular? The commentator of al-Mawaqif replies that the conditional syllogism in its two types, conjunctive and disjunctive, is not inference from universal to particular. 'Rather, the correct view is that the relation between the proof and the proven is either by inclusion (ishtimal) or by entailment (istilzam) without inclusion. This is explicit in conjunctive conditionals and implicit in disjunctive conditionals. As for conditional conjunctive syllogisms, they reduce either to entailment or to inclusion.'
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. Al-Iji tries to reduce Ibn Taymiyya's universal-to-universal inference back to Aristotelian syllogism or analogy. In response, the commentator (of al-Mawaqif) raises a question about conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i): Are they examples of inference from universal to particular? He gives examples: the conjunctive conditional 'Whenever the sun rises, day exists; it is rising' (which yields 'day exists') is a conditional syllogism. The disjunctive 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he will not drown; he is not in the sea, so he will not drown' is a disjunctive syllogism. The commentator argues these are not inference from universal to particular. Instead, the relationship between premise and conclusion is either inclusion (ishtimal) — where the particular is under the universal — or pure entailment (istilzam) without inclusion. Conjunctive conditionals have explicit entailment; disjunctive ones have implicit entailment. So, conditional syllogisms fall under either entailment or inclusion, not directly a universal-to-particular pattern.

Conditional syllogisms rely on either inclusion (ishtimal) or entailment (istilzam), not directly on a universal-to-particular pattern.

Defining Qiyas al-Awla (Argument from the More Fitting)
Ibn Taymiyya defines qiyas al-awla as an argument where the ruling is more fitting in the sub-case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify qiyas al-awla and distinguish it from regular analogy by checking whether the ruling is more deserving in the sub-case.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-awla: if a ruling applies, it applies even more strongly when the cause is clearer.

Ibn Taymiyya introduces a powerful logical tool: qiyas al-awla, where the conclusion is even more deserving than the premise. This goes beyond simple analogy. Let's see how he defines it and why it matters for Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'قياس الأولى' in the Arabic. The term itself means 'measure of the more fitting' or 'argument from the more worthy'.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'قياس' (analogical argument). The differentia is that the required ruling is 'أولى بالثبوت' (more deserving of affirmation) compared to the original case in the proof.
So it is a sub-type of qiyas where the ruling's applicability is stronger in the new case.
3
Contrast with ordinary qiyas
In ordinary qiyas, the ruling applies equally or by extrapolation. In qiyas al-awla, the ruling applies even more strongly because the 'illa (cause) is more evident or intense in the sub-case.
4
Apply the definition to a classic example
The verse 'Do not say to them 'uff'' implies that beating parents is even more forbidden. The prohibition of 'uff' is the original ruling; the sub-case of beating is more deserving of that prohibition.
This is a standard example of qiyas al-awla in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني: قياس الأولى: يعرف ابن تيمية قياس الأولى بأنه ما يكون الحكم المطلوب فيه أولى بالثبوت من الصورة المذكورة في الدليل الدال عليه، ويذكر أن أحمد بن حنبل وغيره من السلف ـ كانوا يستخدمونه. ويحاول ابن تيمية استخدام
The second method: Qiyas al-Awla (the argument from the more fitting). Ibn Taymiyya defines qiyas al-awla as that in which the required ruling is more deserving of affirmation than the form mentioned in the proof indicating it. And he mentions that Ahmad ibn Hanbal and others among the Salaf used to use it. And Ibn Taymiyya attempts to use...
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method, qiyas al-awla. Ibn Taymiyya defines it: the ruling sought is more deserving (awla) of being affirmed than the case mentioned in the evidence. In other words, if a ruling applies in a certain case (the 'proof'), then it applies even more strongly in the target case because the reason is more evident. For example, if Allah says 'Do not say 'uff' to them' (implying not to harm parents), then beating them is even more forbidden — this is qiyas al-awla. Ibn Taymiyya notes that Ahmad ibn Hanbal and the Salaf used this reasoning. The text cuts off, but it indicates Ibn Taymiyya attempted to employ this method.

Key Terms
قياس الأولى

D) 'All humans are mortal, so Zayd is mortal.'

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-awla is a valid argument form where the sub-case is more deserving of the ruling than the original case due to a stronger effective cause ('illa).
