Suhrawardi's Law and the Unification of Moods
Suhrawardi states his law and the reduction method, unifying the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Suhrawardi's law for the second figure and demonstrate how it unifies the four Aristotelian moods.

Core Idea
One law replaces four moods: divergent subjects and reduction to absolute universal affirmative.

After presenting the example, Suhrawardi declares his law: the subjects of the two premises must be so different that the predicate of one cannot be affirmed of the subject of the other. He then says to reduce all propositions to the absolute universal affirmative. This allows him to treat the second figure as having only one valid mood, simplifying Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Comprehend the law of divergent subjects
The subjects of the two premises (e.g., human and stone) must be such that one has a certain property and the other lacks it, making them mutually exclusive in that respect.
This ensures the conclusion that one subject cannot be identical to the other.
2
Understand the reduction to absolute universal affirmative
All propositions, negative or modal, are turned into affirmative ones by absorbing negation and modality into the predicate. For example, 'not capable' becomes 'incapable' as a positive attribute, and 'necessarily capable' becomes 'necessarily-capable' as a single predicate.
This creates a uniform basis for comparison.
3
Apply the law to an Aristotelian mood
Take Cesare (No S is M, All P is M). Reduce 'No S is M' to 'All S is non-M'. Now both premises are universal affirmatives. The subjects S and P have predicates M and non-M, which are contradictory, so they are divergent. Hence the conclusion 'No S is P' follows.
All valid second figure moods reduce to this pattern.
4
Generalize to one mood
Since any valid second figure syllogism, after reduction, fits the pattern of divergent subjects with contradictory predicates, there is no need for four distinct moods. One law covers all.
Suhrawardi thus streamlines the second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the predicate of the other is impossible of relation, like 'Every human is necessarily capable of writing', and 'Every stone is necessarily incapable of writing', so it follows that 'Human is necessarily incapable of being stone'. And this suffices against the lengthy treatment of Aristotelian logic in this figure and its consideration of four moods, whereas in Suhrawardi's logic it is one.

Suhrawardi's Third Figure: Definition and Particular Conclusion
Suhrawardi defines the third figure and demonstrates its particular conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of the categorical syllogism and explain why its conclusion must be particular.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions because predicates may be broader than the middle term.

Suhrawardi defines the third figure by noting that the middle term is the subject in both premises. This structure forces a particular conclusion. Let's explore why by examining his examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In a third figure syllogism, the middle term is the subject of both premises. Find it in the Arabic passage: it is the shared subject ('Zayd' or 'human').
2
Extract the two predicates
The predicates are what are said about the middle term in each premise. In Suhrawardi's example, 'animal' and 'human' (or 'rational') are the predicates.
3
Note the conclusion form
The conclusion links the two predicates, but only particularly: 'Some of P1 is P2'. Suhrawardi states this explicitly: 'some of one predicate is described by the other'.
4
Check the particularity condition
Because one predicate may be more general than the middle term, the conclusion cannot be universal. Suhrawardi explains that 'the predicates may be more general than the subject which is the middle term'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن الشكل الثالث فيقول السهروردي «إذا وجدنا شيئاً واحداً معيناً كالأوسط وصف لمحمولين، علمنا أن شيئاً من أحد المحمولين موصوف بالمحمول الآخر ضرورة». وهذا التعريف للشكل الثالث يتفق مع التعريف الأرسططاليسي له، فالحد الأوسط هو موضوع في المقدمتين. والنتيجة أن شيئاً من المحمولين موصوف بالمحمول الآخر. ومعنى هذا أن النتيجة لا بد أن تكون جزئية. والأمثلة التي يعطيها السهروردي تثبت هذا فمثلاً «أن يكون زيد حيواناً أو زيد إنساناً، علمنا أن شيئاً من الحيوان إنسان. بل وشيء من الإنسان حيوان على أي طريق كان. وإذا كان هذا الشيء المعين معنى عاماً، فيجعل مستغرقاً كقولنا : كل انسان حيوان وكل انسان ناطق . فصار هذا الحصر شيئاً معيناً موصوفاً بالأمرين فيلزم أن يكون شيء من أحدهما هو الآخر». وهذه النتيجة الجزئية هي بعض الحيوان ناطق. بل ان السهروردي يعترف صراحة بأن النتيجة في ضروب الشكل الثالث جزئية فيقول، لا يلزم اتصاف كل واحد من المحمولين بالآخر في هذا السياق. فان المحمولين أو أحدهما، ربما يكون أعم من الموضوع الذي هو الأوسط، والطرف الآخر فلا يلزم اتصاف كل أحدهما بالآخر، بل شيء من أحدهما هو الآخر. وإذا كانت النتيجة جزئية، فلا بد أن تكون احدى المقدمتين جزئية، لأن النتيجة تتبع الأخس من المقدمات. وعلى هذا لا يشترط في هذا الشكل كلية المقدمتين - تبعاً لمذهب السهروردي العام الذي يقرر أن القضايا ينبغي أن تكون كلها كلية - بل تكفي فيه كلية إحدى المقدمتين : كل انسان حيوان وبعض الإنسان كاتب تنتج بعض الحيوان كاتب. يقول السهروردي « وإن كان إحدى المقدمتين مستغرقة والأخرى غير مستغرقة بعد الشركة في الموضوع يجوز ـ فان البعض داخل في الكل فتعين كون الشيء الواحد موصوفاً بالمحمولين، ويلزم اتصاف شيء من أحد المحمولين بالآخر». أما عن السلب، فيتفق السهروردي فيه مع مذهبه العام فيقرر نقله الى المحمول، حتى تصير القضايا كلها موجبة : كل انسان حيوان، وكل إنسان فهو غير الحجر، لينتج بعض الحيوان هو غير الحجر. وإذا ما جعل السلب جزء المحمول كانت القضايا في هذا السياق موجبة. ولم تعد ثمة حاجة الى سلب. وكان من الممكن حمل كل من المحمولين على الموضوع بالإيجاب - ونتج عن هذا نتيجة موجبة : فمثلاً كل إنسان هو لا طائر وكل إنسان هو لا فرس أنتج أن شيئاً مما يوصف بأنه لا طائر هو لا فرس. ويرى السهروردي أننا إذا جعلنا الجهات والسلوب أجزاء للمحمول في المقدمتين استغنينا عن ضروب كثيرة. ثم يقول إن مدار هذا الشكل « هو تيقن اتصاف شيء واحد بشيئين ومخرجه من الشكل الأول أن هذين القولين قضيتان فيهما شيء ما وصف بكلا المحمولين وكل قضيتين فيهما شيء ما وصف بكلا المحمولين . فبعض موصوفات أحد المحمولين يوصف بالآخر، فهذان القولان هكذا حالهما». (١)
As for the third figure, Suhrawardi says: 'If we find one specific thing as the middle term described by two predicates, we know that some of one predicate is described by the other predicate necessarily.' This definition of the third figure agrees with the Aristotelian definition; the middle term is the subject in both premises. The conclusion is that some of one predicate is described by the other predicate. This means that the conclusion must be particular. The examples Suhrawardi gives confirm this; for instance, 'Zayd is an animal' or 'Zayd is a human', we know that some animal is human. Indeed, some human is animal in any case. And if this specific thing is a universal meaning, it is made universal as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'. So this restriction becomes a specific thing described by both attributes, so it follows that some of one is the other. And this particular conclusion is 'some animal is rational'. Moreover, Suhrawardi explicitly acknowledges that the conclusion in the moods of the third figure is particular, saying: 'It is not necessary that every one of the two predicates be described by the other in this context. For the two predicates or one of them may be more general than the subject which is the middle term, and the other extreme, so it is not necessary that every one of them be described by the other; rather, some of one is the other.' And if the conclusion is particular, then one of the premises must be particular, because the conclusion follows the weaker of the premises. Therefore, universality of both premises is not required in this figure—following Suhrawardi's general doctrine that propositions should all be universal—rather, universality of one premise suffices: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is a writer' yields 'Some animal is a writer'. Suhrawardi says: 'If one premise is universal and the other is not universal after sharing the subject, it is permissible—for the part is included in the whole, so it is determined that one thing is described by the two predicates, and it follows that some of one predicate is described by the other.' As for negation, Suhrawardi agrees with his general doctrine and decides to transfer it to the predicate, so that all propositions become affirmative: 'Every human is an animal', and 'Every human is not a stone', producing 'Some animal is not a stone'. And if negation is made part of the predicate, the propositions in this context are affirmative. There is no longer any need for negation. And it was possible to affirm each of the two predicates of the subject positively—and this produced an affirmative conclusion: for example, 'Every human is not-a-bird' and 'Every human is not-a-horse' yielded 'Some of what is described as not-a-bird is not-a-horse'. Suhrawardi holds that if we make modalities and negations parts of the predicate in the two premises, we dispense with many moods. Then he says that the pivot of this figure 'is the certainty that one thing is described by two things, and its extraction from the first figure is that these two statements are propositions in which something is described by both predicates, and every two propositions in which something is described by both predicates—so some of the described things of one predicate is described by the other; these two statements are thus.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of Suhrawardi's treatment of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. He defines the third figure as having the middle term as subject in both premises, leading to a particular conclusion. The author provides examples: from 'Zayd is an animal' and 'Zayd is a human' we deduce 'Some animal is human'. When the middle term is universal, as in 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational', the conclusion is 'Some animal is rational'. Suhrawardi emphasizes that the conclusion must be particular because the predicates may be more general than the middle term. Hence, at least one premise must be particular. He then addresses negation: following his general method, he transfers negation to the predicate, making all propositions affirmative. For instance, 'Every human is not a stone' becomes 'Every human is a non-stone'. This allows affirmative conclusions without separate negative moods. By treating modalities and negations as parts of the predicate, Suhrawardi reduces the number of moods needed. Finally, he links the third figure to the first figure by noting that when one thing is described by two predicates, the conclusion follows by the first figure after appropriate conversion.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the third figure always yield a particular conclusion according to Suhrawardi?
A) The middle term is always particular.
B) The predicates may be more general than the middle term.
C) Both premises must be universal.
D) Negation makes it particular.

Key Takeaway
The third figure forces a particular conclusion because the predicates may be broader than the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Suhrawardi's Negation Shift: Making All Propositions Affirmative
Suhrawardi explains his method of transferring negation to the predicate to keep all propositions affirmative in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a negative proposition into an affirmative one by transferring the negation to the predicate, and apply this to reduce the number of moods in the third figure.

Core Idea
By absorbing negation into the predicate, Suhrawardi eliminates negative propositions and simplifies syllogistic moods.

Suhrawardi proposes a radical simplification: instead of treating negation as a separate operator, he makes it part of the predicate. This way all propositions become affirmative, and many moods become redundant. Let's see how this works in the third figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative proposition
Find the word 'not' or the negative particle in the proposition. For example, 'Every human is not a stone' contains a negation.
2
Transfer negation to the predicate
Move the negation to the predicate term, turning 'not stone' into 'non-stone' or 'غير الحجر' in Arabic.
This preserves the meaning while changing the form.
3
Rewrite as affirmative
The new proposition is affirmative: 'Every human is non-stone'. Now both premises are affirmative.
4
Construct the syllogism with affirmative propositions
Use the converted proposition with another affirmative premise (e.g., 'Every human is non-horse') to produce an affirmative conclusion: 'Some non-stone is non-horse'.
Suhrawardi's example: 'Every human is not-a-bird', 'Every human is not-a-horse' yields 'Some not-a-bird is not-a-horse'.
5
Observe the reduction of moods
Suhrawardi holds that by making modalities and negations parts of the predicate, we dispense with many moods. Why? Because all syllogisms become affirmative, so only moods for affirmative propositions are needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن الشكل الثالث فيقول السهروردي «إذا وجدنا شيئاً واحداً معيناً كالأوسط وصف لمحمولين، علمنا أن شيئاً من أحد المحمولين موصوف بالمحمول الآخر ضرورة». وهذا التعريف للشكل الثالث يتفق مع التعريف الأرسططاليسي له، فالحد الأوسط هو موضوع في المقدمتين. والنتيجة أن شيئاً من المحمولين موصوف بالمحمول الآخر. ومعنى هذا أن النتيجة لا بد أن تكون جزئية. والأمثلة التي يعطيها السهروردي تثبت هذا فمثلاً «أن يكون زيد حيواناً أو زيد إنساناً، علمنا أن شيئاً من الحيوان إنسان. بل وشيء من الإنسان حيوان على أي طريق كان. وإذا كان هذا الشيء المعين معنى عاماً، فيجعل مستغرقاً كقولنا : كل انسان حيوان وكل انسان ناطق . فصار هذا الحصر شيئاً معيناً موصوفاً بالأمرين فيلزم أن يكون شيء من أحدهما هو الآخر». وهذه النتيجة الجزئية هي بعض الحيوان ناطق. بل ان السهروردي يعترف صراحة بأن النتيجة في ضروب الشكل الثالث جزئية فيقول، لا يلزم اتصاف كل واحد من المحمولين بالآخر في هذا السياق. فان المحمولين أو أحدهما، ربما يكون أعم من الموضوع الذي هو الأوسط، والطرف الآخر فلا يلزم اتصاف كل أحدهما بالآخر، بل شيء من أحدهما هو الآخر. وإذا كانت النتيجة جزئية، فلا بد أن تكون احدى المقدمتين جزئية، لأن النتيجة تتبع الأخس من المقدمات. وعلى هذا لا يشترط في هذا الشكل كلية المقدمتين - تبعاً لمذهب السهروردي العام الذي يقرر أن القضايا ينبغي أن تكون كلها كلية - بل تكفي فيه كلية إحدى المقدمتين : كل انسان حيوان وبعض الإنسان كاتب تنتج بعض الحيوان كاتب. يقول السهروردي « وإن كان إحدى المقدمتين مستغرقة والأخرى غير مستغرقة بعد الشركة في الموضوع يجوز ـ فان البعض داخل في الكل فتعين كون الشيء الواحد موصوفاً بالمحمولين، ويلزم اتصاف شيء من أحد المحمولين بالآخر». أما عن السلب، فيتفق السهروردي فيه مع مذهبه العام فيقرر نقله الى المحمول، حتى تصير القضايا كلها موجبة : كل انسان حيوان، وكل إنسان فهو غير الحجر، لينتج بعض الحيوان هو غير الحجر. وإذا ما جعل السلب جزء المحمول كانت القضايا في هذا السياق موجبة. ولم تعد ثمة حاجة الى سلب. وكان من الممكن حمل كل من المحمولين على الموضوع بالإيجاب - ونتج عن هذا نتيجة موجبة : فمثلاً كل إنسان هو لا طائر وكل إنسان هو لا فرس أنتج أن شيئاً مما يوصف بأنه لا طائر هو لا فرس. ويرى السهروردي أننا إذا جعلنا الجهات والسلوب أجزاء للمحمول في المقدمتين استغنينا عن ضروب كثيرة. ثم يقول إن مدار هذا الشكل « هو تيقن اتصاف شيء واحد بشيئين ومخرجه من الشكل الأول أن هذين القولين قضيتان فيهما شيء ما وصف بكلا المحمولين وكل قضيتين فيهما شيء ما وصف بكلا المحمولين . فبعض موصوفات أحد المحمولين يوصف بالآخر، فهذان القولان هكذا حالهما». (١)
As for the third figure, Suhrawardi says: 'If we find one specific thing as the middle term described by two predicates, we know that some of one predicate is described by the other predicate necessarily.' This definition of the third figure agrees with the Aristotelian definition; the middle term is the subject in both premises. The conclusion is that some of one predicate is described by the other predicate. This means that the conclusion must be particular. The examples Suhrawardi gives confirm this; for instance, 'Zayd is an animal' or 'Zayd is a human', we know that some animal is human. Indeed, some human is animal in any case. And if this specific thing is a universal meaning, it is made universal as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational'. So this restriction becomes a specific thing described by both attributes, so it follows that some of one is the other. And this particular conclusion is 'some animal is rational'. Moreover, Suhrawardi explicitly acknowledges that the conclusion in the moods of the third figure is particular, saying: 'It is not necessary that every one of the two predicates be described by the other in this context. For the two predicates or one of them may be more general than the subject which is the middle term, and the other extreme, so it is not necessary that every one of them be described by the other; rather, some of one is the other.' And if the conclusion is particular, then one of the premises must be particular, because the conclusion follows the weaker of the premises. Therefore, universality of both premises is not required in this figure—following Suhrawardi's general doctrine that propositions should all be universal—rather, universality of one premise suffices: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is a writer' yields 'Some animal is a writer'. Suhrawardi says: 'If one premise is universal and the other is not universal after sharing the subject, it is permissible—for the part is included in the whole, so it is determined that one thing is described by the two predicates, and it follows that some of one predicate is described by the other.' As for negation, Suhrawardi agrees with his general doctrine and decides to transfer it to the predicate, so that all propositions become affirmative: 'Every human is an animal', and 'Every human is not a stone', producing 'Some animal is not a stone'. And if negation is made part of the predicate, the propositions in this context are affirmative. There is no longer any need for negation. And it was possible to affirm each of the two predicates of the subject positively—and this produced an affirmative conclusion: for example, 'Every human is not-a-bird' and 'Every human is not-a-horse' yielded 'Some of what is described as not-a-bird is not-a-horse'. Suhrawardi holds that if we make modalities and negations parts of the predicate in the two premises, we dispense with many moods. Then he says that the pivot of this figure 'is the certainty that one thing is described by two things, and its extraction from the first figure is that these two statements are propositions in which something is described by both predicates, and every two propositions in which something is described by both predicates—so some of the described things of one predicate is described by the other; these two statements are thus.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of Suhrawardi's treatment of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. He defines the third figure as having the middle term as subject in both premises, leading to a particular conclusion. The author provides examples: from 'Zayd is an animal' and 'Zayd is a human' we deduce 'Some animal is human'. When the middle term is universal, as in 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is rational', the conclusion is 'Some animal is rational'. Suhrawardi emphasizes that the conclusion must be particular because the predicates may be more general than the middle term. Hence, at least one premise must be particular. He then addresses negation: following his general method, he transfers negation to the predicate, making all propositions affirmative. For instance, 'Every human is not a stone' becomes 'Every human is a non-stone'. This allows affirmative conclusions without separate negative moods. By treating modalities and negations as parts of the predicate, Suhrawardi reduces the number of moods needed. Finally, he links the third figure to the first figure by noting that when one thing is described by two predicates, the conclusion follows by the first figure after appropriate conversion.

Key Terms
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Suhrawardi, what happens when we transfer negation to the predicate?
A) Propositions become negative.
B) Propositions become affirmative.
C) Both premises must be universal.
D) The conclusion becomes universal.

Key Takeaway
By making negation part of the predicate, Suhrawardi converts all propositions to affirmatives, thereby simplifying the syllogistic system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Convert the proposition 'Some animal is not rational' into an affirmative proposition by transferring negation to the predicate. Then use it as the conclusion of a third figure syllogism with appropriate premises.
?
Task 2: Explain in your own words why Suhrawardi says this method 'dispenses with many moods'.

Suhrawardi's Third Figure: Partial Premises Condition
Author presents Suhrawardi's modification of third-figure conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a third-figure categorical syllogism satisfies Suhrawardi's condition of having at least one universal premise when the conclusion is particular.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi shows the third figure needs only one universal premise, not two.

In classical Aristotelian logic, the third figure often requires both premises to be universal to yield a particular conclusion. Suhrawardi, a prominent Islamic logician, challenged this by demonstrating that one universal premise suffices. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications for syllogistic validity in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion's quantity
Look at the conclusion: is it universal or particular? If particular, Suhrawardi's condition applies.
If universal, different conditions may apply.
2
Check the universality of each premise
Determine whether each premise is universal (mustaghriqa) or particular. Mark them as A (universal affirmative), E (universal negative), I (particular affirmative), or O (particular negative).
3
Apply Suhrawardi's rule
According to Suhrawardi, if the conclusion is particular, only one premise needs to be universal. The other can be particular. Verify that the premise pattern meets this (e.g., one A and one I, as in the example).
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take a third-figure syllogism from fiqh or kalam. For instance: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal) and 'Some nabidh is an intoxicant' (particular). Conclusion: 'Some nabidh is forbidden' (particular). Check that one premise is universal.
This satisfies Suhrawardi's condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانت النتيجة جزئية، فلا بد أن تكون احدى المقدمتين جزئية، لأن النتيجة تتبع الأخس من المقدمات. وعلى هذا لا يشترط في هذا الشكل كلية المقدمتين - تبعاً لمذهب السهروردي العام الذي يقرر أن القضايا ينبغي أن تكون كلها كلية - بل تكفي فيه كلية إحدى المقدمتين : كل انسان حيوان وبعض الإنسان كاتب تنتج بعض الحيوان كاتب . يقول السهروردي « وإن كان إحدى المقدمتين مستغرقة والأخرى غير مستغرقة بعد الشركة في الموضوع يجوز ـ فان البعض داخل في الكل فتعين كون الشيء الواحد موصوفاً بالمحمولين ، ويلزم اتصاف شيء من أحد المحمولين بالآخر » أما عن السلب ، فيتفق السهروردي فيه مع مذهبه العام فيقرر نقله الى المحمول ، حتى تصير القضايا كلها موجبة : كل انسان حيوان ، وكل إنسان فهو غير الحجر ، لينتج بعض الحيوان هو غير الحجر وإذا ما جعل السلب جزء المحمول كانت القضايا في هذا السياق موجبة . ولم تعد ثمة حاجة الى سلب . وكان من الممكن حمل كل من المحمولين على الموضوع بالإيجاب - ونتج عن هذا نتيجة موجبة : فمثلاً كل إنسان هو لا طائر وكل إنسان هو لا فرس أنتج أن شيئاً مما يوصف بأنه لا طائر هو لا فرس ويرى السهروردي أننا إذا جعلنا الجهات والسلوب أجزاء للمحمول في المقدمتين استغنينا عن ضروب كثيرة . ثم يقول إن مدار هذا الشكل « هو تيقن اتصاف شيء واحد بشيئين ومخرجه من الشكل الأول أن هذين القولين قضيتان فيهما شيء ما وصف بكلا المحمولين وكل قضيتين فيهما شيء ما وصف بكلا المحمولين . فبعض موصوفات أحد المحمولين يوصف بالآخر ، فهذان القولان هكذا حالهما »(۱) . (۱) شرح ص ١١٣ - ١١٤ .
If the conclusion is particular, one of the two premises must be particular, because the conclusion follows the less [universal] of the premises. And according to this, universality of both premises is not required in this figure—following Suhrawardi's general doctrine that propositions should all be universal—rather, universality of one premise suffices: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is a writer' yields 'Some animal is a writer'. Suhrawardi says: 'If one premise is universal and the other not universal, after the common subject it is permissible—for the particular is included in the universal, so it is determined that one thing is described by both predicates, and it follows that something of one predicate is described by the other.' As for negation, Suhrawardi agrees with his general doctrine and decides to transfer it to the predicate, so that all propositions become affirmative: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is not a stone' yields 'Some animal is not a stone'. If negation is made part of the predicate, the propositions in this context become affirmative. There is no longer any need for negation. And it is possible to affirm both predicates of the subject—and this yields an affirmative result: for example, 'Every human is not a bird' and 'Every human is not a horse' yields 'Something described as not a bird is not a horse'. Suhrawardi holds that if we make modalities and negations parts of the predicate in the two premises, we dispense with many moods. Then he says: 'The pivot of this figure is the certainty that one thing is described by two things, and its derivation from the first figure is that these two statements are two propositions in which something is described by both predicates, and any two propositions in which something is described by both predicates: then some of the ones described by one predicate are described by the other. These two statements are thus.' (1) Explanation pp. 113-114.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage explains Suhrawardi's treatment of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating the general rule: if the conclusion is particular, one premise must be particular. Suhrawardi's innovation is that in the third figure, not both premises need be universal; one universal premise suffices. The example given is: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is a writer' yields 'Some animal is a writer'. Suhrawardi then addresses negative propositions. To avoid dealing with negations, he transfers the negation to the predicate, making all propositions affirmative. For instance, instead of 'No human is a stone', he writes 'Every human is not a stone' (where 'not a stone' is the predicate). This way, the syllogism remains in affirmative form. He extends this to modalities and negations, treating them as parts of the predicate to reduce the number of moods. Finally, Suhrawardi states that the essence of the third figure is the certainty that one thing is described by two predicates, and it derives from the first figure.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
مستغرقة
جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the conclusion of a third-figure syllogism is 'Some animals are writers', which premise must be particular?
A) Both premises must be particular.
B) One premise must be particular, the other can be universal.
C) The premise about 'writers' must be universal.
D) The premise about 'animals' must be particular.

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's innovation: in the third figure, not both premises need be universal; one universal premise suffices when the conclusion is particular.

?
Consider the following syllogism: 'Every act that is commanded is good' (universal) and 'Some acts of prayer are commanded' (particular). Write the conclusion in the third figure and verify that it satisfies Suhrawardi's condition.

Suhrawardi's Transformation of Negative Propositions
Author explains Suhrawardi's method of handling negation in the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a negative categorical proposition into an affirmative one by moving the negation into the predicate, following Suhrawardi's method.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi eliminates negation as a separate operator by absorbing it into the predicate.

Suhrawardi's approach to negation simplifies syllogistic reasoning. Instead of treating negative propositions as a distinct category, he reinterprets them as affirmatives with a negated predicate. This reduces the number of moods and streamlines logical analysis. This lesson demonstrates how to apply this transformation in third-figure syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify a negative proposition in a syllogism
Locate any proposition that uses a negative particle (e.g., 'laysa', 'la'). In standard logic, these are E or O propositions.
Suhrawardi's goal is to eliminate overt negation.
2
Move the negation to the predicate
Reformulate the proposition so that the negation is attached to the predicate term. For example, 'Every human is not a stone' becomes 'Every human is non-stone'. The quantifier and copula remain affirmative.
The new predicate is a negated term (e.g., 'non-stone').
3
Verify the transformed syllogism
Check that the transformed premises still yield the same logical conclusion. For the example, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is non-stone', the conclusion 'Some animal is non-stone' is valid in the third figure.
4
Apply to a new example
Take a third-figure syllogism with two negative premises, e.g., 'Every human is not a bird' and 'Every human is not a horse'. Transform both to affirmatives and derive the conclusion: 'Something described as non-bird is non-horse'.
This avoids the need for special negative moods.

Suhrawardi's Reduction of Conditional Syllogisms
Author states that Suhrawardi parallels conditional syllogisms to categorical ones, thus not repeating their discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Suhrawardi reduces conditional syllogisms to categorical ones, and identify the conditions that make them equivalent.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are just categorical syllogisms in disguise.

Suhrawardi was known for simplifying logic. He noticed that conditional syllogisms (if-then) share the same validity conditions as categorical syllogisms. Therefore, he did not treat them separately. This lesson explores that insight and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of syllogisms
Distinguish between categorical (subject-predicate) and conditional (if-then) syllogisms. In categorical syllogisms, premises are of the form 'Every A is B'. In conditional syllogisms, premises are of the form 'If P then Q'.
2
Understand Suhrawardi's claim
Suhrawardi states that the conditions (shara'it) of conditional syllogisms resemble those of categorical syllogisms. Conditions refer to rules for validity, such as mood and figure requirements.
3
Test with an example
Take a conditional syllogism from Kalam: 'If the world is contingent, it has a cause. The world is contingent, therefore it has a cause.' Convert it to categorical form: 'Every contingent thing has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause.' Notice the middle term is 'contingent' in both.
4
Reflect on the reduction
Why did Suhrawardi not repeat the discussion? Because the validity conditions are already covered in categorical logic. This shows his reductionist approach—he aims to unify logical forms under one set of rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن القياسات الشرطية فيقرر السهروردي أن شرائطها تشبه شرائط القياس الحملي . ولذلك لم يجد السهروردي داعياً لتكرار بحثها(۱) . (۱) شرح ص ١١٤ - ١١٦ .
As for conditional syllogisms, Suhrawardi states that their conditions resemble the conditions of categorical syllogism. Therefore, Suhrawardi found no need to repeat their discussion. (1) Explanation pp. 114-116.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This short passage notes Suhrawardi's view that conditional syllogisms share conditions with categorical ones. Because of this similarity, Suhrawardi did not see the need to discuss conditional syllogisms separately. This is consistent with his reductionist approach to logic, where he aims to simplify and unify logical forms.

Suhrawardi's Reduction of Propositions to al-Battata
Describes Suhrawardi's reduction of propositions to universal affirmative perpetual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Suhrawardi's reduction of all propositions to the universal affirmative perpetual and identify its implications for syllogistic.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi reduced all propositions to a single type: the perpetual universal affirmative.

In this passage, we learn about Suhrawardi's bold logical innovation: he condensed all propositional types into one fundamental form—the universal affirmative perpetual (al-battata). This reduction had far-reaching consequences for the theory of syllogism, moving it away from Greek models. Let us explore how he achieved this and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational proposition type
Locate in the passage the proposition that Suhrawardi made the basis of his logic: the universal affirmative perpetual (al-battata). Note that it is a universal affirmative statement with no temporal restriction.
2
Recognize the status of other proposition types
Note that Suhrawardi considered particular propositions useful only in limited contexts: conversion, contradiction, and some syllogistic moods. All other propositions are reduced to the battata type.
3
Understand the impact on syllogistic
Because all propositions are essentially universal affirmative perpetuals, syllogistic theory becomes simpler. Suhrawardi did not need to treat conditional syllogisms separately; their conditions resemble categorical ones.
4
Compare with Greek logic
Aristotelian logic recognized multiple propositional forms. Suhrawardi's reduction caused a departure from Greek investigations in many topics of syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن القياسات الشرطية فيقرر السهروردي أن شرائطها تشبه شرائط القياس الحملي . ولذلك لم يجد السهروردي داعياً لتكرار بحثها . والآن تبين لنا أن رد القضايا إلى القضية الكلية الموجبة البتاتة ، كان له أثر كبير على مباحث القياس ، وأنه نتج عن هذا خروج تلك المباحث في كثير من أبحاثها عن المباحث اليونانية . تلك هي محاولة السهروردي المنطقية ، وهي محاولة من أعمق المحاولات في تاريخ المنطق على العموم ، لا نجد لها مثيلاً لدى مفكري الإسلام ، ولا لدى مفكري اليونان من قبلهم . فقد وضع كما رأينا مبحثاً في الحد، من المرجح أنه لم يتأثر فيه الى حد ما بمؤثرات خارجية ، ثم وضع مبحثاً في القضايا حاول أن يرد فيه جميع القضايا الى القضية التي أسماها بالبتاتة . ولم يعترف بفائدة ما للقضية الجزئية إلا في بعض نواحي العكس والتناقص وبعض ضروب الأقيسة فغير بهذا كثيراً من أصول المنطق الأرسططاليسي . والسهروردي يشبه - إلى حد كبير - المناطقة الرياضيين المعاصرين في أوروبة الذين حاولوا اختصار منطق أرسطو . غير أننا يجب أن نلاحظ أن نقد السهروردي للمنطق الأرسططاليسي من ناحية ، ومنطقة الجديد من ناحية أخرى لا يمثل الصوفية في شيء ، لأنه نقد المنطق من ناحية عقلية ووضع منطقاً عقلياً . بينما الصوفية لا يعترفون بأي صورة من صور الفكر العقلي ولا ينقدون المنطق الأرسططاليسي أو أي صورة من صور التفكير من وجهة منطقية نظرية ، ولكن من ناحية أنه يخالف في جوهره طريق الصوفية وهو الكشف أو الذوق . وبهذا ينتهي بحثنا لموقف مفكري الإسلام - متكلمين أو أصوليين أو فقهاء أو صوفية أو فلاسفة من المنطق الأرسططاليسي وغيره . . وسنحاول في الصفحات القادمة أن نبين انتقال هذا المنهج الى دائرة العلماء .
As for conditional syllogisms, Suhrawardi determines that their conditions resemble the conditions of categorical syllogisms. Therefore, Suhrawardi did not find a need to repeat their discussion. And now it has become clear to us that the reduction of propositions to the universal affirmative perpetual had a great impact on the topics of syllogism, and that this resulted in those topics, in many of their investigations, departing from Greek investigations. That is Suhrawardi's logical attempt, and it is one of the deepest attempts in the history of logic in general; we do not find for it an equivalent among the thinkers of Islam, nor among the thinkers of the Greeks before them. For he established, as we saw, a study on definition (al-hadd), which it is likely that he was not influenced in it to some extent by external influences. Then he established a study on propositions in which he attempted to reduce all propositions to the proposition he called 'al-battata' (the perpetual). And he did not recognize any benefit of the particular proposition except in some aspects of conversion and contradiction and some moods of syllogisms. Thus he changed many of the principles of Aristotelian logic. And Suhrawardi highly resembles the contemporary mathematical logicians in Europe who attempted to condense Aristotle's logic. However, we must note that Suhrawardi's critique of Aristotelian logic on the one hand, and his new logic on the other hand, do not represent Sufism at all, because he critiqued logic from a rational perspective and established a rational logic. While the Sufis do not recognize any form of rational thought and do not critique Aristotelian logic or any form of thinking from a logical theoretical perspective, but from the perspective that it contradicts in its essence the path of Sufism, which is unveiling (kashf) or taste (dhawq). And with this our study of the position of Islamic thinkers—theologians, legal theorists, jurists, Sufis, or philosophers—on Aristotelian logic and others ends... And we will attempt in the coming pages to show the transmission of this method to the circle of scholars.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical reforms of Suhrawardi. It begins by stating that Suhrawardi treats conditional syllogisms similarly to categorical ones and therefore sees no need for a separate discussion. Then it explains that Suhrawardi's reduction of all propositions to the universal affirmative perpetual (al-battata) significantly impacted the study of syllogism, moving it away from Greek sources. The author considers Suhrawardi's attempt one of the deepest in the history of logic, unmatched by Islamic or Greek thinkers. Suhrawardi developed a theory of definition (hadd) that was relatively independent of external influences, and a theory of propositions that reduced all to the battata type. He only acknowledged the utility of particular propositions in conversion, contradiction, and some syllogistic moods, thus altering many Aristotelian logical principles. The author compares Suhrawardi to modern European mathematical logicians who sought to condense Aristotle's logic. However, the author notes that Suhrawardi's critique of Aristotelian logic and his new logic are rational, not Sufi, because Sufis reject rational thought and criticize logic only insofar as it contradicts their path of unveiling (kashf) and taste (dhawq). The passage concludes the survey of Islamic thinkers' positions on Aristotelian logic and announces a forthcoming discussion on how this method transmitted to the circle of scholars.

Key Terms
القضية الكلية الموجبة البتاتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of proposition did Suhrawardi make the foundation of his logical system?
A) The particular affirmative
B) The universal affirmative perpetual (al-battata)
C) The universal negative
D) The conditional

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's reduction of all propositions to the universal affirmative perpetual (al-battata) streamlined syllogistic theory and marked a significant departure from Aristotelian logic.

Suhrawardi's Rational Critique vs. Sufi Rejection of Logic
Distinguishes Suhrawardi's rational critique from Sufi rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Suhrawardi's rational critique of Aristotelian logic from the Sufi rejection rooted in mystical epistemology.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi's logical reforms were rational, not mystical, rejecting Sufi anti-rationalism.

A common error is to associate Suhrawardi with Sufi mysticism in his logical work. The passage explicitly clarifies that his critique of Aristotelian logic was a rational, theoretical endeavor, while Sufis reject logic because it contradicts their path of unveiling (kashf) and taste (dhawq). This lesson helps you see the epistemic distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of Suhrawardi's critique
The passage says Suhrawardi critiqued logic 'from a rational perspective' and established a rational logic. He is not criticizing logic from a mystical standpoint.
2
Contrast with the Sufi position
Sufis, according to the passage, do not recognize any form of rational thought. They reject logic not on logical grounds but because it contradicts the Sufi path of unveiling (kashf) and taste (dhawq).
3
Note the similarity to modern logicians
The author compares Suhrawardi's condensation of Aristotle to contemporary mathematical logicians in Europe who also attempted to simplify Aristotelian logic—a rational enterprise.
4
Assess why Suhrawardi's logic is not Sufi
The passage explicitly states that his critique and new logic 'do not represent Sufism at all' because he operated within rational thought, whereas Sufism rejects rational thought in principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
